Qiyas Justifying the Verse's Precedence over Rulings
Explanation of qiyas justifying textual order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qiyas structure (asl, far', illa) used to justify the textual ordering of the wudu verse before its rulings.

Core Idea
The verse is the root; rulings are branches; the root must precede the branch in rank and mention.

The commentator explains why the author placed the wudu verse (5:6) at the beginning of the section, ahead of the detailed rulings. The justification is a clear example of qiyas jali: the verse is the evidence (dalil) serving as the root (asl), and the rulings that follow are its branches (far'). By a simple logical chain, what is the root must be mentioned first. This lesson walks you through that reasoning and addresses a key objection regarding explicit textual support for some of the Hanafi fara'id.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of the qiyas
The verse (آية الوضوء) is the asl (root) because it provides the evidence. The rulings (ahkam) are the far' (branch) because they depend on the verse.
The commentator explicitly states: 'هذه الآية دليل لما يأتي من الأحكام والدليل أصل الحكم، وهو فرعه'
2
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the evidence is the origin (أصل) and the ruling is its derivative (فرع). Being the origin necessitates precedence in rank (الرتبة).
This is a jali illa because it is directly stated: 'الدليل أصل الحكم'.
3
Apply the hidden premise
The commentator adds an implicit premise: whatever precedes in rank should also be mentioned first, to match natural disposition (ليوافق الوضع الطبع). This completes the qiyas.
This premise is 'مقدمة مطوية' – an unstated but logical assumption.
4
Analyze the objection about wiping the beard
An objector notes that the verse does not explicitly mention wiping one quarter of the beard, a Hanafi fard. The commentator offers two responses: (1) the verse indicates it by inference (استنباط), or (2) the word 'fara'id' is generic (جنس) due to the lam of genus, so it does not require each individual act to be mentioned.
This shows how Hanafi usul handles apparent gaps between the scriptural source and derived rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١ ] قوله : ولأن الدليل ... الخ ; تقريره على نمط القياس أن هذه الآية دليل لما يأتي من الأحكام والدليل أصل الحكم، وهو فرعه فينتج أن هذه الآية أصل لما يأتي، وهو فرعه. ثم يضم إليه أن كل أصل مقدَّم على الفرع بالرتبة، فينتج أنّ هذه الآية مقدمة على الأحكام التي تأتي بالرتبة، ثم تضمّ معه مقدّمة مطوية، وهي أن كل ما يكون مقدماً على الشيء رتبة ينبغي أن يُقَدَّمَ عليه ذكراً ; ليوافق الوضع الطبع. [٢] قوله : ثم ; الغرض منه بيان وجه إدخال المصنّف الفاء على قوله : «فرض الوضوء»، وإيراد «ثم» هاهنا لمجرد التأخير الذكري والرتبي. [٣] قوله : على فرائض الوضوء ; فيه إشارة إلى أن قول المصنف فيما يأتي «وسنته»، وقوله : «ومستحبّة» ليس معطوفاً على قوله : «فرض الوضوء»، فإنّ الآية لا تدل على السنن والمستحبات حتى يصح إيرادُ فاء التعقيب عليها، بل هما إما جملتان مبتدأتان، وإما معطوفتان على قوله : ففرض الوضوء. فإن قلت : من الفرائض التي يذكرها المصنّف مسح ربع اللحية، وهذه الآية لا تدلّ عليه. قلت : الدلالة أعم من أن تكون على سبيل الصراحة، وعلى سبيل الاستنباط، أو يقال : ليس المراد بالفرائض جميعها، بل جنسها ، والجمعية بطلت بدخول لام الجنس.
11. His statement: 'And because the evidence is the root...' etc. Its explanation in the pattern of analogy is that this verse is evidence for the rulings that follow, and the evidence is the root of the ruling, and the ruling is its branch. This yields that this verse is the root for what follows, which is its branch. Then one adds that every root precedes the branch in rank, resulting in this verse preceding the rulings that follow in rank. Then one adds an implicit premise: that whatever precedes something in rank should be mentioned before it, to match natural disposition. [2] His statement: 'then' (thumma) – its purpose is to explain the reason the author inserted the fa' of accompaniment in his words 'fard al-wudu', and the use of 'thumma' here is merely for temporal and rank-based delay. [3] His statement: 'on the obligatory acts of wudu' – it indicates that the author's upcoming statement 'its sunnah' and 'its mustahabb' are not conjoined to his statement 'fard al-wudu', because the verse does not indicate the sunan and mustahabbat so that placing the fa' of accompaniment upon them would be correct. Rather, they are either independent sentences or conjoined to his statement 'fa fard al-wudu'. If you say: among the fara'id that the author mentions is wiping one quarter of the beard, and this verse does not indicate it. I reply: indication is broader than that it be explicit; it can be by inference, or it can be said that what is meant by 'fara'id' is not all of them, but their genus, and the plural is negated by the lam of genus.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the author's statement 'ولأن الدليل أصل' (and because the evidence is the root). The commentator analyzes it as an analogy (qiyas): the verse (آية الوضوء) is the evidence (dalil), which is the root (asl), and the rulings (ahkam) that follow are the branches (far'). The root precedes the branch in rank, and what precedes in rank should be mentioned first. This justifies the author placing the verse before the rulings. The commentary then explains the use of the word 'ثم' (then) to indicate delay in mention and rank, not temporal sequence. It also clarifies that the verse only indicates the obligatory acts (fara'id) of wudu, not the sunnan or mustahabbat, so when the author later mentions 'wa sunnatuhu' and 'wa mustahabbuhu', these are not connected by the particle fa' (indicating immediate sequence) but are separate statements. Finally, it addresses an objection: the verse does not explicitly mention wiping one quarter of the beard (a Hanafi fard). The response is that the verse indicates it either by explicit text or by inference (istinbat), or that the term 'fara'id' is generic (jins) and the plural does not imply all individuals due to the lam of genus.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here, the pattern (namat) of qiyas is used to prove the order of the text.
الأصل (al-aṣl): The source or root; here, the verse as the evidence from which rulings are derived.
الفرع (al-far‘): The branch or derivative; here, the rulings of wudu that depend on the verse.
فاء التعقيب (fā’ al-ta‘qīb): The letter fa used to indicate immediate sequence (ta‘qib).
لام الجنس (lām al-jins): The lam that indicates genus; it negates the implication of all individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator justify the author's placement of the verse before the rulings?
A) By quoting a hadith that requires it.
B) By an analogy (qiyas) where the verse is the root and the rulings are branches.
C) By claiming it is a universal scholarly consensus.
D) By stating it is for blessing only.

Key Takeaway
The verse is the evidence (asl); rulings are its branches (far'); the asl must precede the far' in textual order by qiyas jali.

The Verse as Origin of Wudu Obligations
Explaining why the Quranic verse is the basis for wudu obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Quranic verse (5:6) is considered the original proof (asl) for the obligations of wudu in the Hanafi school and identify the role of fa al-ta'qib in this context.

Core Idea
The verse is the origin; the rulings are its branches — the origin is prioritized in rank and mention.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'aya explains why the author of Al-Wiqaya placed the Quranic verse before listing the obligations of wudu. The commentator uses a qiyas-like reasoning to show that the verse is the original proof (asl) and the obligations are its branches (far'). Understanding this relationship helps you see how the Hanafi school derives rulings from Quranic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asl-far' relationship
The commentator establishes that the Quranic verse is the origin (asl) and the subsequent obligations are branches (far'). This is the foundational structure of the argument.
The term 'asl' here means original proof, not a legal maxim.
2
Understand the ranking premise
The origin is prioritized over the branch in rank (ruta), so it should be mentioned first. This justifies placing the verse before the list of obligations in the matn.
The commentator adds a hidden premise: things prioritized in rank should be prioritized in mention.
3
Analyze the use of 'fa'
The letter 'fa' (fa al-ta'qib) indicates immediate sequence. The text uses 'fa' to show that the obligations directly follow from the verse, reinforcing the cause-effect relationship.
The commentator distinguishes between the 'fa' used here and the word 'thumma' which indicates delay.
4
Address the beard objection
The passage answers a potential objection: the matn includes wiping the beard as an obligation, but the verse does not mention it explicitly. The response is that the verse indicates obligations either explicitly or by derivation (istinbat), or that 'fara'id' refers to the genus of obligations.
This shows how Hanafi scholars expand the scope of Quranic evidence through usul.

Book of Purity: Wudu. And because the evidence is the origin, and the ruling is its branch, and the origin is prioritized in rank over the branch. Then [؟] the verse was indicative of the obligations of wudu, so he inserted the fa of sequence in his statement: [؟]. And the answer: that what is intended by 'al-tayammun' (beginning with the right) is in the beginning of speech, not absolutely, and al-tayammun is evidence for absolute beginning, not restricted; and in it is apparent weakness. It is better to say: that the tanwin in 'al-tayammun' is for the type, and what is intended is al-tayammun in wording and meaning together. And verbal al-tayammun, even if it occurs for other than it, the meaningful one is specific to it, because it contains the meaning of washing that purifies from actual and legal impurities; thus its mention is used as a means to become washed from base sins. [11] His statement: 'And because the evidence... etc.' Its formulation in the pattern of qiyas is that this verse is evidence for the rulings that follow, and the evidence is the origin of the ruling, and it is its branch; thus it follows that this verse is the origin for what follows, and it is its branch. Then he adds to it that every origin is prioritized over the branch in rank, so it follows that this verse is prioritized over the rulings that follow in rank. Then he adds with it a hidden premise: that everything that is prioritized over something in rank should be prioritized over it in mention, so as to conform to natural placement. [2] His statement: 'then' — the purpose of it is to explain the reason for the author's insertion of the fa on his statement 'obligation of wudu', and the bringing of 'thumma' here is merely for delay in mention and rank. [3] His statement: 'upon the obligations of wudu' — in it is an indication that the author's subsequent statement 'and its sunnah' and his statement 'and its mustahabbat' are not conjuncts to his statement 'obligation of wudu', for the verse does not indicate the sunan and mustahabbat such that the insertion of fa of sequence upon them is correct. Rather, they are either two independent sentences or conjuncts to his statement: 'so the obligation of wudu'. If you say: Among the obligations that the author mentions is wiping a quarter of the beard, and this verse does not indicate it. I say: Indication is more general than being explicit or being by derivation; or it is said: what is meant by 'fara'id' is not all of them, but their genus, and the collectivity is invalidated by the entry of the lam of the genus.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion on the reasoning behind the arrangement of the matn in Al-Wiqaya. The commentator explains why the Quranic verse (Surah Al-Ma'idah 5:6) is placed first before listing the obligations of wudu. The reasoning is that the verse is the original proof (asl) for the rulings, and the rulings are its branches (far'). Just as an origin is prioritized over its branch in rank, so too should it be prioritized in mention. The commentator also addresses the insertion of the letter 'fa' (indicating sequence) in the text, stating that it shows the verse immediately leads to the obligations. A subtle point is made about the word 'al-tayammun' (seeking blessing by beginning with the right), clarifying that the intended meaning is both literal and figurative. The passage then addresses a potential objection: the matn includes wiping a quarter of the beard as an obligation, but the verse does not explicitly mention it. The response is that the verse indicates obligations either explicitly or by derivation (istinbat), or that the word 'fara'id' in the text refers to the genus of obligations, not every individual one, as indicated by the lam al-jins.

Key Terms
التيمن (al-tayammun): In this passage, it refers to beginning with the right or auspicious, meant both verbally and meaningfully.
فاء التعقيب (fa' al-ta'qib): The letter 'fa' that indicates immediate sequence or consequence, showing that the verse directly leads to the obligations.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse on wudu is the origin (asl) for all its obligations, and the Hanafi school uses this asl-far' reasoning to prioritize it in mention and derive additional rulings.

The Hanafi Definition of 'Fard' in Wudu: Pillar vs. Definitive Proof
Defining 'fard' and 'ghasl' in the matn of Al-Wiqaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fard as rukn and fard as established by qat'i dalil, and state the implications for deniability.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'fard' in wudu means indispensable pillar, not necessarily based on absolute proof.

When you read 'fard' in classical Hanafi fiqh texts, it can carry different shades of meaning. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that in the context of wudu, 'fard' refers to the essential pillar (rukn) without which the act is invalid. It does not necessarily mean an obligation established by an absolute proof (qat'i dalil), denial of which would be kufr. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly evaluating the status of disputed obligations like wiping a quarter of the head.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
Locate the term 'fard' in the source text and note that it is being defined specifically in relation to wudu. The commentator explicitly states that the intended meaning here is 'that which is indispensable from the aspect of being a pillar (rukn).'
This is not the only meaning of fard in Hanafi usul, but it is the relevant one for this passage.
2
Distinguish between two meanings of fard
Compare the definition given here with the alternative: fard as 'that which is established by a definitive proof without doubt.' The passage explicitly rejects the latter for the acts of wudu (like washing the feet and wiping a quarter of the head) because otherwise their deniers would be kafir, which no one claims.
This distinction is foundational for understanding the Hanafi concept of fard 'amali (practical) vs. fard i'tiqadi (doctrinal).
3
Apply the distinction to the four fard acts of wudu
Realize that while the four acts (face, hands, head, feet) are all fard in the sense of rukn, only some (like washing the face and hands) may be based on more definitive proof. The passage specifically mentions that washing the feet, washing the hands with elbows, and wiping a quarter of the head are 'not like that' (i.e., not based on qat'i dalil), so denying them does not constitute kufr.
This explains why there is legitimate ikhtilaf among scholars on the exact extent of wiping the head, and why Hanafis do not declare each other kafir for differing.
4
Understand the linguistic nuance of 'ghasl'
The passage then shifts to explain the term 'ghasl' (washing) with different vowel patterns: ghasl (fatha = the act), ghusl (damma = full-body wash), and ghis (kasrah = the washing agent). This is a linguistic clarification of the word used in the matn.
This part is less central but reinforces the importance of precise terminology in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ففرض الوضوء (۱) ؛ المراد بالفرض هاهنا ما لا بُدَّ منه في الوضوء، من حيث كونه ركناً له، لا ما ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه على ما يفهم من «البناية»(۲) وذخيرة العقبى» (۳) وغيرهما ؛ فإنّ غسل الرجلين وغسل اليدين مع المرفقين، ومسح
(٤)
ربع الرأس واللحية ليس كذلك، وإلا يلزم أن يكون منكره كافراً، ولم يقل به أحد (4). [٢]قوله : غسل) ؛ هو بالفتح بمعنى : إزالة الوسخ ونحوه، بإمرار الماء، وأمّا الغُسلُ بالضم فهو اسم من الاغتسال، وهو غسلُ تمام الجسد، وبالكسر اسم لما يغسلُ
(۱) بالضم مصدر من الوضاءة بمعنى الحسن ، وقد جعل في الشرع اسماً لغسل الأعضاء الثلاثة ومسح الرأس، وأما بالفتح هو اسم لما يتوضأ به ينظر: «المغرب»(ص٤٨٩).
(۲) «البناية» (١) : ١٠٤).
(۳) «ذخيرة العقبی»(ص۷)، وفيه : «الفرض اصطلاحاً ما ثبت بدليل قطع لا شبهة فيه، وحكمه أن يستحق العقاب تاركه بلا عذر ويكره جاحده لا يقال من جملة الفرائض مسح ربع الرأس، ويكفر جاحده، ولا يأثم، بل يثاب ؛ لأنه مجتهد فيه كمالك والشافعي والحسن البصري ؛ ؛ لأنا نقول : الجاهل من لا يكون مؤولاً ، وكل من هؤلاء الأجلاء الذين يقولون بعضهم بالاستيعاب، وبعضهم بالأقل كالشعرة والشعرتين، وبعضهم بالأكثر لا يعد جاحداً ؛ لأنه مؤول كذا فهم من تقرير الأكمل في «شرح الهداية» ». (٤) أقول : ما عرف به اللكنوي الفرض لطيف دقيق ، ولكن لازم التعريف الآخر غير لازم، فقد عرف الفرض بهذا غير واحد وبين في حكمه الكفر بالإنكار في المتفق عليه كما في «مقدمة الصلاة» (ص ۲۱)، قال عبد الغني النابلسي في «الجوهر الكلي شرح عمدة المصلي» (ق ۲ / ب – ۱/۳ ) : أي على فرضيته يعني الاعتقادي دون الفرض العملي ؛ لأن العملي ما تفوت الصحة بفوته : كالوتر تفوت بفوته صحة صلاة الفجر للمتذكر له، وكمسح ربع الرأس، وكل فرض مختلف فيه بين المجتهدين». كما بينت ذلك في «المرقاة شرح مقدمة الصلاة» (ص ۲۱). (٥) الغسل بالفتح مصدر غسل، والفتح أشهر وأفصح عند أهل اللغة، وبالضم استعمله أكثر الفقهاء. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر» (۱ : ۲۱).
His statement: 'So the obligation of wudu (1)'; what is meant by 'fard' here is that which is indispensable in wudu, from the aspect of it being a pillar (rukn) for it, not that which is established by a definitive proof without doubt, as understood from 'al-Binaya' (2) and 'Dhakirat al-'Uqba' (3) and others; for washing the feet and washing the hands together with the elbows, and wiping (4) a quarter of the head and beard are not like that; otherwise it would necessitate that its denier be a disbeliever, and no one has said that (4). [2] His statement: 'washing (ghasl)' — it is with the fatha meaning: removal of dirt and the like, by running water. As for 'ghusl' with dammah, it is a noun from 'ghtisal', which is washing the entire body. And with kasrah, it is a noun for what is washed with. (1) With dammah, it is a source from 'wada'ah' meaning beauty, and it has been made in the Shari'ah a name for washing the three limbs and wiping the head; as for with fatha, it is a name for what one performs wudu with. See: 'al-Mughrib' (p. 489). (2) 'al-Binaya' (1:104). (3) 'Dhakirat al-'Uqba' (p. 7), in it: 'Fard technically is what is established by a definitive proof without doubt, and its ruling is that its abandoner without excuse deserves punishment and its denier is not labelled a disbeliever; it is not said that among the obligations is wiping a quarter of the head, and its denier becomes a disbeliever; rather, he does not sin but is rewarded because it is a matter of ijtihad, like Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Hasan al-Basri. For we say: The ignorant person is not one who makes ta'wil; all of these noble figures, some of whom say [wiping] entirely, some say the minimum like one hair or two, and some say more, are not considered deniers because they make ta'wil.' This is understood from the report of al-Akmal in 'Sharh al-Hidaya'.' (4) I say: What al-Laknawi defined as fard is subtle and precise, but the necessary implication of the other definition is not necessary; for many have defined fard in this way and explained in its ruling that denial leads to disbelief in what is agreed upon, as in 'Muqaddimat al-Salah' (p. 21). 'Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi said in 'al-Jawhar al-Kulli Sharh 'Umdat al-Musalli' (folio 2/b–1/3): 'It means upon its obligatoriness, i.e., doctrinal (i'tiqadi) as opposed to practical (amali); because the practical is that whose omission causes validity to be lost: like witr, whose omission loses the validity of the Fajr prayer for the one who remembers it, and like wiping a quarter of the head; and every fard that is disputed among mujtahids.' As I explained in 'al-Marqat Sharh Muqaddimat al-Salah' (p. 21). (5) 'Ghasl' with fatha is a source of 'ghasala', and the fatha is more famous and eloquent among language scholars; with dammah it is used by most jurists. See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1:21).
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the term 'fard' as used in the context of wudu. The commentator explains that here 'fard' means the essential act (rukn) that is indispensable for the validity of wudu. This is distinguished from the broader technical meaning of 'fard' as something established by a definitive proof (dalil qat'i), denial of which would entail disbelief. The commentator notes that acts like washing the feet, washing the hands and arms, and wiping a quarter of the head are not established by such definitive proof among the Hanafis themselves; thus, denying these does not make one a disbeliever. This is a crucial point for distinguishing between fard 'amali (practical obligation, where scholarly disagreement exists) and fard i'tiqadi (doctrinal obligation, where the proof is qat'i and denial is kufr). The passage then moves to the linguistic meaning of 'ghasl' (washing). With fatha on the ghayn, it is the verbal noun meaning the act of removing dirt by running water. With dammah, 'ghusl' refers to washing the entire body (ghusl). With kasrah, it refers to the substance used for washing. The footnotes provide additional references from Hanafi works like al-Binaya, Dhakirat al-'Uqba, and Majma' al-Anhur, reinforcing the distinction.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): In this passage, fard means the essential act (rukn) without which wudu is invalid; it is distinguished from fard as defined by a qat'i dalil.
الغسل (al-ghasl): With fatha, it is the verbal noun meaning the act of washing by running water to remove impurities. With dammah (ghusl), it refers to full-body washing. With kasrah, it is the washing agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the meaning of 'fard' in the phrase 'ففرض الوضوء'?
A) Something proven by a definitive proof without doubt
B) The indispensable pillar (rukn) of wudu
C) An act that is recommended (sunnah)
D) An act that is permissible (mubah)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the term 'fard' in the context of wudu refers to the indispensable pillar (rukn), not necessarily an obligation proven by a definitive text.

Definition of Fard in Wudu and Boundaries of the Face
Commentary defining 'fard' for wudu and boundaries of the face.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'fard' in wudu, identify the exact boundaries of the face, and explain the evidence for excluding the ears.

Core Idea
The face extends from the natural hairline to the chin, and from ear to ear; the ears are part of the head, not the face.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator clarifies that 'fard' in the context of wudu means an essential pillar (rukn), not necessarily one proven by a decisive text. He then carefully defines the boundaries of the face, addressing potential misunderstandings about the hairline and the inclusion of the ears. This lesson dissects that definition and the evidence behind it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafi usage of 'fard'
Recognize that 'fard' here refers to a required component (rukn) indispensable for wudu, not a ruling with qat'i evidence. This nuance allows some obligatory acts (like wiping the head) to be established by zanni proofs.
Other schools may equate fard with qat'i; this is a distinctive Hanafi position.
2
Identify the upper boundary of the face
The text defines the upper limit as 'from the hair' (min al-sha'r), specifically the hairline (al-qisas) of the head, excluding beard and mustache. This includes the natural growth area even if hair is absent.
The term 'qisas' refers to the parting or growth line; it ensures the boundary is fixed regardless of baldness.
3
Determine the lateral and lower boundaries
The face ends at the ears laterally; the lower boundary is the chin. The ears are excluded based on the hadith 'The ears are part of the head,' so they are wiped, not washed as part of the face.
The hadith is reported by Ibn Majah and others; it is ahad but accepted in practice.
4
Apply the definition to edge cases
For a bald person, the area where hair normally grows must still be washed. Similarly, if hair recedes from the temples due to illness, the exposed area is still part of the face because it is the natural growth place.
This avoids confusion: the boundary is defined by the natural growth line, not by actual hair presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففرض الوضوء : غسل الوجه من الشعر . قوله : ففرض الوضوء؛ المراد بالفرض هاهنا ما لا بُدَّ منه في الوضوء، من حيث كونه ركناً له، لا ما ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه. قوله : غسل؛ هو بالفتح بمعنى : إزالة الوسخ ونحوه، بإمرار الماء. قوله : أي من قصاص؛ هو بالضمّ مقطع الشعر، ومنتهى منبته من مقدم الرأس أو حواليه. والغرض منه دفع ما يراد أن قوله : «من الشعر» لا يصلح لتحديد الوجه، فأشار إلى أنَّ المضاف إليه محذوف، أي شعر الرأس احتراز عن شعر اللحية والشارب، والمراد به شعر ينبت مواجه الوجه خلاف جانب القفاء. ولمَّا كان يُتوهم أنه قد لا يكون على الرأس شعر، فكيف يصح التحديد به، أضاف إليه لفظ : «القصاص» إشارة إلى أن المراد منتهى منبت الشعر، سواء نبت أو لا. قوله : وهو؛ هذا تفسير للقصاص، والمراد منتهى خروج الشعر على مقتضى الطبيعة والعادة، فلا يرد الأصلع : وهو من انحسر شعر مقدَّم رأسه لمرض وغيره، وكذا النزعتان؛ أعني جانبا الجبهة إذا انحسر الشعر عنهما لمرض، لا يجب غسلهما في الوضوء؛ لأنهما محلّ نبات الشعر بمقتضى الطبع، وإن لم ينبت لعارض. قوله : إلى الأذن؛ بضمتين، وبضم الهمزة وسكون الذال، والغاية هاهنا خارجة عن المغيا، دلّ عليه حديث النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : الأذنان من الرأس. أخرجه ابن ماجه
The obligatory part of wudu is washing the face from the hair. His statement: 'The obligatory part of wudu': what is meant by 'obligatory' here is that which is indispensable in wudu, in terms of being a pillar of it, not that which is established by a decisive proof free from doubt. His statement: 'washing' – it is with a fatha meaning removal of dirt and the like by passing water. His statement: 'i.e., from the parting' – it is with a damma meaning the cut of the hair, its growth end from the front of the head or around it. The purpose is to rebut the understanding that his statement 'from the hair' is not suitable for defining the face. So he indicated that the added noun is omitted, i.e., the hair of the head, to exclude beard and mustache hair, and what is meant is the hair that grows facing the face, unlike the back of the head. And since it might be thought that there may be no hair on the head, how then can one define it? He added the word 'the parting' to indicate that what is meant is the end of the hair growth, whether it grows or not. His statement: 'and it is' – this is an interpretation of 'the parting', and what is meant is the end of the hair's emergence according to nature and habit. Thus it does not apply to the bald person, i.e., one whose front head hair has receded due to illness or other reason, and likewise the two temples; meaning the two sides of the forehead if hair recedes from them due to illness, it is not obligatory to wash them in wudu because they are the place of hair growth by nature, even if it did not grow due to a hindrance. His statement: 'to the ear' – with a double damma, and with a hamza damma and sukoon on the dhal, the limit here is external to what is limited, as indicated by the Prophet's hadith: 'The ears are part of the head.' Reported by Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn of Al-Wiqayah. It defines the obligatory (fard) acts of wudu as a whole and specifically the washing of the face. The commentator first clarifies that 'fard' here does not mean a ruling proven by decisive proof (qat'i) but rather refers to a pillar (rukn) that is essential for wudu's validity. This is important because some obligatory acts like washing the feet and wiping the head are also fard but are established by proof open to interpretation. Next, he explains the word 'ghusl' (washing) as the removal of impurity by running water. Then, he discusses the boundaries of the face as defined by 'from the hair' (min al-sha'r). To avoid confusion, he explains that 'hair' here specifically means the hair on the head, excluding beard and mustache. He introduces the term 'al-qisas' (the hairline) to indicate the natural growth point of hair, emphasizing that the boundary is the growth line even if the hair has fallen out. Thus, a bald person must wash the area where hair would normally grow. He then discusses the ears, citing the hadith that 'the ears are part of the head,' which places them outside the face limit; washing the ears is part of wiping the head, not the face. So the face extends from the hairline to the chin and from ear to ear, with the ears excluded.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): In this context, 'fard' means an essential pillar (rukn) of wudu, not necessarily one established by a decisive proof that would make its denier a disbeliever.
الغسل (al-ghusl (with fatha)): Washing: removing dirt or impurity by passing water over the body part.
القصاص (al-qisas): The parting of hair, meaning the natural hairline or the boundary where hair grows on the front of the head.
الأذن (al-udhun): The ear, which is part of the head and thus excluded from the face in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the phrase 'min al-sha'r' (from the hair) refer to in defining the face?
A) The beard and mustache hair
B) The hair of the head, specifically the growth line
C) Any visible hair on the face
D) The hair on the back of the head

Key Takeaway
Washing the face from the natural hairline to the chin and ear to ear is a fard (rukn) of wudu, with the ears excluded because they belong to the head.

The Width of the Face in Wudu: From Hairline to Ear
Commentary defining the width of the face and addressing an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the width of the face in wudu, explain the evidence for including the area before the ear, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding washing the white area for a bearded man.

Core Idea
The face in wudu extends from ear to ear; the ears themselves are from the head, making the ear the boundary.

This passage from a commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the precise width of the face (wajh) for the obligation of washing in wudu. The width is defined from the hairline (qisas) to the ear, and the key proof is the hadith 'al-udhunani min al-ra's', which places the ears as part of the head, not the face. An intra-Hanafi dispute arises over whether a bearded man must wash the white skin between ear and sideburn; the relied-upon position requires it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the width boundary
The face's width is from the hairline (min qisas sha'ri al-ra's) to the ear. Understand 'qisas' as the edge of hair growth, which remains the boundary even for a bald person.
The term 'qisas' refers to the natural place of hair growth, not actual hair.
2
Understand the hadith proof
The hadith 'al-udhunani min al-ra's' proves that the ears are part of the head, so they serve as the outer limit of the face. Everything before the ear (including the white area) is part of the face.
The hadith is hasan according to Ibn al-Humam.
3
Note the exception for baldness
The definition uses 'qisas' to refer to the natural hair growth boundary, so a bald person's face boundary remains the same even without hair.
The text explicitly excludes the bald man from any leniency.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that washing the white area between ear and sideburn is fard for everyone. Abu Yusuf exempts a bearded man from reaching that skin.
The ikhtilaf is only about the bearded man; all agree on the boundary for others.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The fatwa is on the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as stated in al-Sirajiyyah and other sources. Therefore, washing that area is obligatory for all.
The mufta bihi is the majority opinion within the Hanafi school.

The book of purification: wudu to the ear. (i.e., from the hair edge of the head) (the ending of the hair growth of the head (to the ear)) by which the head is washed with hibiscus and other things. Likewise in 'al-Mughrib'. He said: i.e., from qisas; it is with damm the cutting place of hair, and the ending of its growth from the front of the head or around it. So the idafah is 'ahdiya, so it is not objected that this interpretation applies to the back of the neck. And since it might be imagined that there may be no hair on the head, so how can the definition be valid with it? He added the word 'al-qisas' indicating that what is meant is the ending of the hair growth, whether it grows or not. He said: 'Huwa'; this is an interpretation of qisas, and what is meant is the ending of hair emergence according to nature and habit. So the bald man is not included: he is one whose front head hair has receded due to illness or other, and likewise the two temples, i.e., the sides of the forehead if hair recedes from them due to illness: it is not obligatory to wash them in wudu because they are the place of hair growth by nature, even if it did not grow due to an obstacle. He said: 'Fayakun'; this is a branch on his saying: 'Ila al-udhun': meaning when he limited the face widthwise to the ear, it is understood from that that everything before the ear is included in the face. So the white area between the ear and the 'idhar' - which is with kasr of the important 'ayn, the side of the beard - is included in the face. So washing it is fard, and this is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and from Abu Yusuf he does not consider it necessary for the bearded man to make water reach it, and the fatwa is on the first, as in 'al-Sirajiyyah' and others. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the face (wajh) for wudu, specifically its width boundary. The commentator explains that the matn's phrase 'from the hair edge of the head' (min qisas sha'ri al-ra's) refers to the edge where hair growth begins, not the hair itself. This is to exclude the beard and mustache and to ensure the definition works even for a bald person. The term 'qisas' means the cutting place of hair, and the intended boundary is the natural hairline. Then the commentator discusses the phrase 'to the ear' (ila al-udhun). He clarifies that the ear is the limit, meaning the face ends at the ear; the ear itself is part of the head, as proven by the hadith 'al-udhunani min al-ra's' (the ears are from the head). This hadith has a hasan chain. The commentator also addresses a potential question: why did the author mention the width before the length? He explains that in practice, water from the forehead first reaches the ears, then flows to the chin, so the width is mentioned first. The key ruling is that the face includes the area between the ear and the sideburn (al-'idhar), known as the white area. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, washing this area is obligatory (fard). Abu Yusuf relieved a bearded man from this obligation, but the fatwa follows the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). The passage ends with a brief biography of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, a prominent Hanafi scholar, clarifying the correct spelling of his name.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): The edge of the hair growth, i.e., the hairline where the head hair begins.
عذار ('idhar): The side of the face alongside the beard, the area between the ear and the beard.
الوجه (al-wajh): The face; for wudu, its length is from the hairline to the chin, and its width from ear to ear.

Ruling on the Area Between Ear and Cheek in Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation to wash the area between ear and cheek.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing the area between the ear and cheek and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The area between ear and cheek is part of the face; washing it is fard.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle but important detail of wudu: the area between the ear and the cheek, known as al-‘idhār. While the definition of the face widthwise includes this area, a difference arose among the early Hanafi imams on whether a bearded man must wash it. We will explore the majority view and the counter-opinion of Abu Yusuf, and see which position is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Locate the disputed area
Identify the anatomical region: the area between the ear and the cheek, called al-‘idhār. This is the white spot many neglect.
The face extends widthwise from ear to ear, so this area is within the boundary.
2
State the majority Hanafi ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, this area is part of the face and must be washed – it is fard (obligatory).
This is the view of the majority of Hanafi scholars.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf exempts the bearded man (al-multahī) from making water reach that area, citing difficulty.
His view is a minority within the school.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'الفتوى على الأوّل' – the fatwa is on the first view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). This is the relied-upon position.
Sources like al-Sirajiyyah confirm this tarjih.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author, after defining the face from the forehead to the chin and widthwise to the ears, explains that the area between the ear and the cheek (العذار) is part of the face. Therefore, washing it is obligatory (fard) in wudu. He states this is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the majority of Hanafi scholars. He then reports Abu Yusuf's position: for a man with a beard, it is not necessary to make water reach that area. However, the relied-upon fatwa is the first view, as stated in al-Sirajiyyah and other works. The practical consequence: a person must ensure water reaches the white spot between the ear and the cheek.

Key Terms
العذار (al-‘idhār): The side of the beard, i.e., the cheek area between the ear and the chin.
الملتحي (al-multahī): A man who has a beard (meaning an adult male with facial hair).

Requirement of Water Flow and the Third Opinion for the Ear-Cheek Area
Intra-Hanafi dispute on water flow condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on water flow (isālah) in wudu and explain the practical dispensation for the area between the ear and cheek according to Shams al-A'imma's third opinion.

Core Idea
Water flow is required for all limbs except the ear-cheek area, where mere wetting suffices due to hardship.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a critical refinement by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani regarding the condition of water flow in wudu. While the established Zahir al-Riwayah position requires water to flow or drip from all washed limbs, Abu Yusuf held it was not necessary. Shams al-A'imma derived a third, middle view: flow is required for all limbs except the area between the ear and cheek, where wetting alone is sufficient. This lesson unpacks the three opinions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zahir al-Riwayah position
The standard Hanafi condition: water must flow or drip from every washed limb, including the area between ear and cheek.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Recognize Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf considered water flow not a condition at all for any limb; mere wetting suffices.
His view is a minority within the school.
3
Understand Shams al-A'imma's third opinion
Shams al-A'imma synthesized the two: flow is required for all limbs except the ear-cheek area, where wetting is sufficient due to hardship.
This is a later refinement (اختراع) not from the founding Imams.
4
Examine the basis of the exception
The hardship and difficulty of ensuring water flow on the curved area between ear and cheek justifies the dispensation, based on the principle of raf' al-haraj.
The narration from Abu Yusuf is used as evidence.
5
Apply the opinion in practice
For all limbs except the ear-cheek area, ensure water flows; for that area, simply wetting it is sufficient for valid wudu.
This is the operative ruling (mufta bihi) for ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكفيه أن يَبُلُّ ما بين العذار والأذن، ولا يجب إسالة الماء عليه؛ واخترع شمس الأئمة منهما قولاً ثالثاً، وهو أن المعتبر في جميع الأعضاء المغسولة إسالة الماء إلا فيما بين العذار والأذن، فإنّه يكفيه أن يبله بالماء، وإن لم يتقاطر، حيث قال أكثر مشايخنا على وجوب غسل ما بين العذار والأذن، إلا أن فيه كلفة ومشقة، فالأولى أن يقال: تكفيه بلّة الماء، بناءً على ما روي عن أبي يوسف. كذا نقله عنه في «المحيط». فالأقوال هاهنا صارت ثلاثة: أحدها: ما ذهب إليه أبو حنيفة ومحمد من أن المعتبر في الغسل هو الإسالة والتقاطر في جميع الأعضاء المغسولة وأجزائها، وأن ما بين العذار والأذن داخل في الوجه، فيفترض غسله: كغسل باقي الأعضاء.
It suffices for him to wet what is between the cheek and the ear, and water flow is not obligatory upon it. And Shams al-A'imma originated from them a third opinion: that what is considered in all washed limbs is water flow except for what is between the cheek and the ear, for wetting it with water suffices, even if it does not drip. Since most of our scholars hold the obligation of washing what is between the cheek and the ear, but there is hardship and difficulty in it, so it is better to say: wetting it suffices, based on what is narrated from Abu Yusuf. Thus, it is reported from him in al-Muhit. So the opinions here become three: One of them: what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad held, that what is considered in washing is flow and dripping in all washed limbs and their parts, and that what is between the cheek and the ear is included in the face, so its washing is obligatory like the washing of the other limbs.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a refinement by Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani regarding the requirement of water flow (isālah) in wudu. The established ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah is that water must flow or drip from the washed limbs for wudu to be valid. Abu Yusuf held that flow is not a condition. Shams al-A'imma derived a third view: for all limbs, flow is required except for the area between the ear and the cheek. For that area, merely wetting (ball) is sufficient, even if water does not drip, due to the hardship and difficulty of ensuring flow there. This third view is based on a narration from Abu Yusuf and is reported in al-Muhit al-Burhani. The three opinions are thus: (1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: flow required for all parts including that area. (2) Abu Yusuf's original: flow not required at all. (3) Shams al-A'imma's position: flow required except for that area. The text only presents the first opinion in detail for comparison, but the third opinion is highlighted as a practical concession.

Key Terms
إسالة الماء (isālat al-mā'): Making water flow or drip from the washed limb; the condition that water must run off the limb during wudu.
البلّ (al-ball): Wetting; simply making the area moist without requiring flow or dripping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Water flow is not required for any limb
C) Water flow is required for all limbs except that area, where wetting suffices
D) That area does not need to be washed at all

Key Takeaway
The requirement of water flow in wudu is subject to intra-Hanafi refinement; exceptional hardship permits mere wetting for the ear-cheek area.

Water Flow vs. Wetting in Wudu: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on necessity of water flow vs mere wetting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on washing between cheek and ear, explain the linguistic basis for the muftā bihi, and apply the ruling to a wudu scenario.

Core Idea
Water must flow over the face including the cheek-ear area; mere wetting does not satisfy washing (ghusl).

The commentary from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah tackles a specific intra-madhhab dispute: when performing wudu, is it sufficient to simply wet the area between the cheek and ear, or must water actually flow over it? The author presents three views within the Hanafi school, clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'washing', and resolves the disagreement, showing why the majority position requiring water flow is the one relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Recognize the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (flow required for whole face), Abu Yusuf (wetting sufficient, but later clarified to require minimal drip), and Shams al-A'imma (flow except for cheek-ear area).
The author labels them as first, second, and third positions.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
Understand that the decisive proof is the consensus of Arabic language experts that 'ghusl' (washing) inherently involves passing and flowing water, not mere wetting.
This linguistic basis is cited by the author to support the first position.
3
Evaluate the response to Abu Yusuf's narration
The author explains that the reported view of Abu Yusuf was misinterpreted; his intent was that dripping of a drop or two suffices, not complete absence of flow, thus aligning with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This refutation undermines Shams al-A'imma's reliance on that narration.
4
Determine the muftā bihi and its practical effect
Conclude that the operative ruling is the first position: water must flow over the entire face including the cheek-ear area. This affects the validity of wudu.
The author states 'al-muftā bihi min hādhihi al-aqwāl huwa al-qawl al-awwal'.

Source Text

يكفيه أن يَبُلُّ ما بين العِذار والأذن ، ولا يجب إسالة الماء عليه ؛ بناءً على ما رُوي عن أبي يوسف : أنَّ المصلي إذا بَلَّ وجهَهُ وأعضاء وضوئه بالماء، ولم يسل الماء.

هذا
The book of purification: Wudu. It suffices him to wet the area between the cheek and the ear, and it is not obligatory to cause water to flow upon it. And in any case, his attribution is to al-Halwa, and his father was among those who sold halwa, and it is possible, assuming the nun (nunation), that the ha is damma, for al-hulwan means sweetness, mentioned in al-Qamus. I have clarified that investigation and mentioned his biography in the introduction and in al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya. As for what 'Abd Allah al-Harawi mentioned in his glosses on the Sharh and Akhi Jalbī in Dhakhirat al-'Uqba: that it is attribution to Hulwan (with damma), the name of a town in rural Iraq, it is false, for Shams al-A'imma is not from there. His statement: 'it suffices him'; know that causing water to flow from the limbs is a condition in the apparent narration, and it is not permissible to perform wudu unless water drips from the washed limbs. And from Abu Yusuf, it is not a condition. So in al-Dhakhira and others. And Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani devised a third opinion: that what is considered in all washed limbs is the flowing of water, except in the area between the cheek and the ear, for it suffices him to wet it with water, even if it does not drip, because most of our mashayikh hold the obligation of washing the area between the cheek and the ear, but there is hardship and difficulty in it, so it is better to say: his wetting with water suffices, based on what was narrated from Abu Yusuf. Thus it is transmitted from him in al-Muhit. The statements here become three: One: what Abu Hanifa and Muhammad held: that what is considered in washing is the flowing and dripping in all washed limbs and their parts, and that the area between the cheek and the ear is included in the face, so its washing is obligatory like the washing of the rest of the limbs. The second: the statement of Abu Yusuf that it suffices in washing to wet even if water does not flow, and that the area between the cheek and the ear is not included in the face, so its wetting is also not obligatory. The third: the statement of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani: that what is considered in washing the face and others is the flowing, except the area between the cheek and the ear, for despite its inclusion in the face, wetting suffices. The mufta bihi among these statements is the first statement, for the people of language unanimously consider passing and flowing in the reality of washing, and wetting is not washing according to them, and similarly they interpreted the face as what is encountered, and the area between the cheek and the ear is likewise. His statement: 'based on what was narrated from Abu Yusuf'; its apparent is that it is not a madhhab for him, but a narration from him, but the reliable books are corroborated in mentioning the disagreement between him and them regarding this discussion. If you say: This narrated from Abu Yusuf implies that wetting all limbs suffices, and there is no specificity for the area between the cheek and the ear, so how can the statement of Shams al-A'imma be based upon it? Also, washing that mentioned area is not obligatory according to Abu Yusuf, either because it is not included in the definition of the face as they attributed to him, or because its washing drops although it is included in the face out of necessity, as some fuqaha mentioned. So what is the basis of building upon his statement? I say: Al-Halwani is counted among the mujtahids, so by his ijtihad he chose their statement that the mentioned area is part of the face and the statement of Abu Yusuf that wetting suffices in this area, due to the verification of hardship in washing it and the lack of exertion in wetting. His statement: 'but it was said...'; thus it is mentioned in al-Dhakhira, and the purpose of the istidrak is to refute Shams al-A'imma by saying that the intent of Abu Yusuf in what was narrated from him of the sufficiency of wetting is that he does not require abundant dripping, rather dripping of one or two drops suffices, not that dripping is not required at all. Then, the madhhab of Abu Yusuf agrees with the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so the building of Shams al-A'imma of his statement upon it is not valid. This.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of water flow (isālah) versus mere wetting (ball) in wudu, specifically concerning the area between the cheek and ear (mā bayna al-ʿidhār wa al-udhun). The author, from the commentary ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah, discusses three positions within the Hanafi school. The first position, held by Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, is that washing (ghusl) requires water to flow and drip from all washed limbs, and the area between the cheek and ear is part of the face, so it must be washed with flowing water. This is the relied-upon position (muftā bihi) because linguistically, washing (ghusl) entails passing water and causing it to flow, not merely wetting. The second position is attributed to Imam Abu Yusuf, who reportedly held that mere wetting suffices for all limbs, and that the area between cheek and ear is not part of the face, so even wetting is not obligatory. However, the author notes that Abu Yusuf's view in the reliable books is actually different: he does not require abundant dripping, but minimal dripping (a drop or two) is sufficient, thus aligning with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The third position is that of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, who compromised: the whole face requires flowing water except the area between cheek and ear, where wetting suffices due to hardship. This view is criticized because it builds on a misunderstood narration from Abu Yusuf. The author clarifies that the correct understanding of Abu Yusuf's narration is that he does require some dripping, just not profuse, so al-Halwani's basis is invalid. Therefore, the operative fatwa is the first position: water must flow over the entire face including the area between cheek and ear.

Key Terms
إسالة (isālah): Causing water to flow over the limb, as distinct from merely wetting (ball). In this text, it is considered the essence of ghusl (washing).
بل (ball): Wetting without causing water to flow or drip. In this text, it is contrasted with isālah; ball alone does not constitute valid ghusl according to the relied-upon position.
عذار (ʿidhār): The cheek area, specifically the part between the ear and the side of the face. The text discusses the area between the ʿidhār and the ear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (muftā bihi) position in this passage, what is required for washing the area between the cheek and ear in wudu?
A) Mere wetting (ball) suffices.
B) Water must flow (isālah) and drip.
C) Neither wetting nor flow is required because it is not part of the face.
D) It is recommended but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi muftā bihi requires water to flow over the entire face, including the cheek-ear area; mere wetting does not fulfill the obligation of washing in wudu.

Washing the Area Between the Sideburn and Ear in Wudu: Bal vs Isala
Ruling on whether mere wetting suffices for the area between sideburn and ear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether mere wetting (bal) suffices for the area between the sideburn and ear in wudu, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The area between the sideburn and ear is part of the face; water must flow (isala) over it during wudu — mere wetting is insufficient.

In wudu, washing the face is a fard obligation. But what exactly constitutes 'washing'? Does merely wetting the skin suffice, or must water flow? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses this question specifically for the area between the sideburn and ear, presenting an intra-Hanafi dispute and clarifying the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? The washing of the area between the sideburn and ear during wudu. The ruling is that it is wajib to wash with isala (flowing water).
This area is part of the face; the face must be washed in wudu.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the consensus of Arabic linguists that the reality of ghusl (washing) requires passing and flowing of water. Mere wetting (bal) is not considered ghusl.
This linguistic consensus is the primary proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
Since ghusl linguistically entails isala, the shar'i obligation to wash the face (Quran 5:6) must be fulfilled with isala. Bal does not satisfy the requirement.
The Quranic command 'wash your faces' is interpreted according to the linguistic definition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
Within the Hanafi school, there is a narration from Abu Yusuf that bal suffices, and Shams al-A'imma restricted this concession to the sideburn area due to hardship. However, the majority interpret Abu Yusuf's view as requiring minimal flow.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhahib.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred ruling)
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that isala is required for all parts of the face, including this area. Minimal flow (a drop or two) is sufficient.
Al-Halwani's ijtihad combined the rulings: the area is part of the face, but the concession in the narration is interpreted as minimal flow.

Source Text

[٢]قوله : لكن قيل ... الخ ؛ هكذا ذكر في «الذخيرة»، والمقصود من الاستدراك الردّ على شمس الأئمة الله بأن مراد أبي يوسف الله فيما روى عنه من كفاية البل أنه لا يشترط التقاطر الكثير ، بل يكفيه تقاطر قطرة أو قطرتين، لا أنه لا يشترط التقاطر مطلقاً. وحينئذ يوافق مذهب أبي يوسف الله مذهب أبي حنيفة ومحمد فلا يصح بناء شمس الأئمة له قوله عليه. وقطرة أو قطرتين؛ إنما ذكره بحرف الترديد لتخالف العبارات في ذكر مذهب أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، فظاهر عامة الكتب أن سيلان القطرة الواحدة كاف، والمفهوم من بعضها اشتراط القطرتين. وأسفل الذقن؛ هو بفتحتين مجتمع العظمين اللذين هما منبتا الأسنان. كذا في «القاموس)، وهو معطوف على قوله : الأذن، وخارج من المغيا إن أريد من الأسفل ما تحت الذقن، وداخل فيه إن أريد به الجزء الآخر من الذقن.
It suffices for him to wet what is between the sideburn and the ear, and it is not obligatory to cause water to flow upon it; based on what was narrated from Abu Yusuf: that if a person praying wets his face and limbs of his ablution with water and the water does not flow, it is permissible. But it was said: its interpretation: and the second: the statement of Abu Yusuf that it suffices in washing to wet even if water does not flow, and that what is between the sideburn and ear is not included in the face, so its wetting is also not obligatory. And the third: the statement of Shams al-A'imma that the criterion in washing the face and other parts is the flowing (isala) except what is between the sideburn and ear, for although it is included in the face, mere wetting suffices. And the fatwa-worthy among these statements is the first statement, for the linguists are unanimous on considering passing and flowing in the reality of washing, and wetting is not washing according to them, and likewise they interpreted the face as that by which one faces (others) and what is between the sideburn and ear similarly. [1] His statement: based on what was narrated from Abu Yusuf; its apparent meaning is that it is not his (established) madhhab, but rather a narration from him, but the reliable books are numerous in mentioning the difference between him and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) in this research. If you say: this narrated from Abu Yusuf necessitates that wetting suffices for all limbs, with no specificity for what is between the sideburn and ear, so how can the statement of Shams al-A'imma be built upon it? Also, washing the mentioned part is not obligatory according to Abu Yusuf either because it is not included within the limit of the face as they attribute to him, or because its washing is dropped even if it is within the face due to necessity, as some jurists mentioned. So what is the basis for building upon his statement? I say: al-Halwani is counted among the mujtahids; by his ijtihad he chose their statement regarding the mentioned part being from the face and the statement of Abu Yusuf regarding the sufficiency of wetting in this part; due to the hardship of washing it and not coming out in wetting. [2] His statement: but it was said... etc.; thus it is mentioned in al-Dhakhira, and the purpose of the exception is to refute Shams al-A'imma by saying that the meaning of Abu Yusuf in what was narrated from him about sufficiency of wetting is that a large dropping is not required, rather the dropping of one or two drops suffices him, not that dropping is not required at all. Then the madhhab of Abu Yusuf agrees with the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, so the building of Shams al-A'imma upon his statement is not valid. And one drop or two drops; he only mentioned it with the letter of alternation due to the difference of expressions in mentioning the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, for the apparent of most books is that the flow of a single drop is sufficient, and the understanding from some is the requirement of two drops. And the lower chin; it is, with two fatha, the meeting place of the two bones which are the origins of the teeth. Thus in al-Qamus, and it is conjoined to his statement: the ear, and it is outside the (phrase 'what is between') if by 'lower' is meant what is under the chin, and inside it if by it is meant the other part of the chin.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning wudu, specifically whether mere wetting (bal) of the area between the sideburn (al-'idhar) and the ear (al-udhun) suffices, or whether water must flow (isala). The text presents three positions: First, the mufta bihi (fatwa-worthy) opinion, which requires isala for the entire face including this area, citing the consensus of linguists that 'ghusl' (washing) entails passing water and its flow, not mere wetting. Second, an opinion attributed to Abu Yusuf that wetting suffices, with two interpretations: either he did not consider this area part of the face, or he allowed wetting due to hardship. Third, a compromise by Shams al-A'imma: isala is required for the face except this specific area where bal suffices despite being part of the face. The text then refutes Shams al-A'imma's position by arguing that the narration from Abu Yusuf, when correctly understood, actually agrees with the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad—that isala is required but a minimal flow (drop or two) is sufficient. Thus the relied-upon position is that isala is essential for all washable parts in wudu. The passage also clarifies the definition of the face's boundary, specifically the lower chin (asfal al-dhaqn), explaining its inclusion or exclusion in the 'what is between' (al-mughaya) phrase.

Key Terms
البل (al-bal): Mere wetting without water flowing or running off the limb.
الإسالة (al-isala): Causing water to flow or run over the limb so that it falls off.
العذار (al-'idhar): The sideburn, the area of hair between the ear and the cheekbone.
أسفل الذقن (asfal al-dhaqn): The lower part of the chin, specifically the meeting point of the two jawbones where the teeth are rooted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school, what is required for washing the area between the sideburn and ear in wudu?
A) Mere wetting (bal) is sufficient because it is not part of the face.
B) Water must flow (isala) because it is part of the face and ghusl requires flow.
C) It is not required to wash this area at all.
D) It is sufficient to wipe over it without water.

Key Takeaway
The area between the sideburn and ear is part of the face; washing it in wudu requires water to flow (isala), not mere wetting (bal).

Inclusion of Elbows and Ankles in Wudu Washing
Ruling that elbows and ankles are included in washing hands and feet in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the elbows and ankles must be washed in wudu, citing the Quranic verse and the hadith of Jabir, and distinguish this position from the view of Zufar.

Core Idea
Elbows and ankles are included in washing; 'ila' indicates inclusion, not exclusion.

In wudu, the command to wash the hands 'to the elbows' and the feet 'to the ankles' raises a question: are the elbows and ankles themselves required to be washed, or is it sufficient to wash up to them? The Hanafi school, based on a linguistic principle and a Prophetic practice, rules that they must be included. This brief passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the proof and addresses a dissenting opinion within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses washing the hands and feet in wudu. The ruling is that washing includes the elbows and ankles.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (5:6) commands washing 'to the elbows' and 'to the ankles'. The Hanafis interpret 'ila' (to) as inclusive of the limit.
The verse is qat'i in transmission but zanni in interpretation.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, the word 'ila' in a limit context includes the limit itself. Thus, washing the hands must cover the elbows, and washing the feet must cover the ankles.
This is based on the principle that the limit is included in the limited (al-mughaya yadkhulu fi al-ghaya).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, held that the elbows and ankles are not included. Malik also has a narration supporting this view.
The majority of the school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) disagree with Zufar.
5
Examine the additional evidence
The passage cites a hadith from Jabir that the Prophet made water flow over his elbows during wudu, supporting inclusion.
This hadith is reported by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عَطَفَ على الوجه قوله : ( واليدين والرجلين مع المرفقين ، والكعبين) خلافاً لزفر ، فإنَّ عنده لا يَدْخُلُ المرفقان والكعبان في الغسل ; وإيماء إلى دخول الغاية في قوله : ( وَأَيْدِيكُمْ إِلَى الْمَرَافِقِ ) ، وقوله : ( وَأَرْجُلَكُمْ إِلَى الْكَعْبَينِ ) في المغيا، وقد ورد عن جابر كما أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي أن النبي : كان إذا توضأ أدار الماء على مرفقيه. خلافاً لزفر : أي حال كون هذا الحكم مخالفاً لزفر، أو خالف هذا الحكم خلافاً لزفر وكذا لمالك في رواية، والأخرى عنه مثل ما ذهب إليه أئمتنا، وبه قال الشافعي وأحمد كذا ذكره العيني.
Then he conjoined to the face his statement: (and the two hands and the two feet with the two elbows and the two ankles) contrary to Zufar, for according to him the two elbows and the two ankles are not included in the washing; and a suggestion of the inclusion of the limit (ghaya) in his statement: (and your hands up to the elbows) and his statement: (and your feet up to the ankles) in the thing included (al-mughaya), and it has been reported from Jabir as al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi narrated that the Prophet, when he performed wudu, made water flow over his elbows. Contrary to Zufar: meaning that this ruling is contrary to Zufar, or this ruling contrary to Zufar and also Malik in one narration, and the other narration from him is like what our imams went to, and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said it—thus mentioned by al-'Ayni.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the obligation to wash the elbows and ankles as part of the hands and feet in wudu. The commentator explains that the matn explicitly includes the elbows and ankles with the hands and feet ('with the two elbows and the two ankles'). He notes that this is contrary to the position of Zufar (a Hanafi scholar), who held that the elbows and ankles are not included in the washing. The proof for the Hanafi position is twofold: First, the Quranic verse (al-Ma'idah 5:6) commands washing 'to the elbows' and 'to the ankles', and the word 'ila' (to) in the verse is interpreted by the Hanafis as including the limit (i.e., the elbows and ankles themselves must be washed), not merely ending before them. Second, a hadith from Jabir (reported by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi) shows that the Prophet would make water flow over his elbows while performing wudu. The commentary also notes that Malik has two narrations—one agreeing with Zufar, another agreeing with the Hanafi position—while al-Shafi'i and Ahmad agree with the Hanafi school on this point.

Key Terms
المرفقان (al-mirfaqan): The two elbows, the joints connecting the upper arm and forearm.
الكعبان (al-ka'ban): The two ankle bones, the protruding bones on each side of the ankle joint.
الغاية (al-ghaya): The limit or boundary mentioned in the verse ('to the elbows', 'to the ankles').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The elbows are not required to be washed because they are not part of the hand.
B) The elbows must be washed because they are included in the hand based on the Quranic verse.
C) It is sufficient to wash only the forearms up to the elbows.
D) The elbows are washed only if water flows over them naturally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates washing the elbows and ankles as part of the hands and feet in wudu, based on the inclusive interpretation of 'ila' and supported by the Prophet's practice.

Defining the Face: The Lower Chin in Wudu
Definition of face and inclusion of lower chin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the face in wudu and explain why the lower chin is included.

Core Idea
The lower chin is an integral part of the face in Hanafi wudu.

The boundaries of the face in wudu are crucial because washing the face is a fard (obligation). The passage clarifies that the lower chin—the meeting place of the two jawbones—completes the face from all four sides. Two interpretations exist: 'below the chin' may refer to the area under the chin or the lower part of the chin itself. The Hanafi school adopts the inclusive view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and definition
Locate the phrase 'أسفل الذقن' and understand its anatomical meaning: the meeting of the two bones that are the beds of the teeth.
This is the key term for the face boundary.
2
Determine the legal implication
Decide whether this area is part of the face that must be washed. The text says it completes the boundaries from the four sides.
3
Analyze the two interpretations
The commentary notes that 'below the chin' could mean the area under the chin (outside the face) or the lower part of the chin itself (inside the face). Understand both readings.
The distinction depends on what 'asfal' refers to.
4
Conclude with the Hanafi position
The adopted view is that the lower chin is included as part of the face, because the definition in al-Qamus and the author's wording support inclusion.
This is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة الوضوء
وأسفل الذقن، واليدين والرجلين مع المرفقين ، والكعبين
،
أنه سال من العضو قطرة أو قطرتان، ولم يتدارك. وأسفل الذقن " فتمم حدود الوجه من الأطراف الأربعة.
٢٤٣
ثم عَطَفَ على الوجه قوله : ( واليدين والرجلين مع المرفقين ، والكعبين) خلافاً لزفر ، فإنَّ عنده لا يَدْخُلُ المرفقان والكعبان في الغسل ؛
(۳)
وإنما ذكر هذا التأويل بلفظ : «قيل» ؛ لكونه مخالفا لما عليه كلام الفقهاء من ذكر الخلاف بينه وبينهما، وتصريح أن التقاطر عنده ليس بشرط مطلقاً، لكن لا يخفى أنه
لولا هذا التأويل فقول أبي يوسف الا الله مخالف للغة والشرع ؛ ولذا لم يأخذوا به. [١]قوله : قطرة أو قطرتان ؛ إنما ذكره بحرف الترديد لتخالف العبارات في ذكر مذهب أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، فظاهر عامة الكتب أن سيلان القطرة الواحدة كاف، والمفهوم من بعضها اشتراط القطرتين.
(1)

Book of Purity, Wudu. And below the chin, and the hands and feet with the elbows and ankles. That a drop or two drops flowed from the limb and were not caught up. And below the chin completes the boundaries of the face from the four sides. Then he conjoined to the face his saying: (and the hands and feet with the elbows and ankles) contrary to Zufar, for according to him the elbows and ankles are not included in the washing. And he only mentioned this interpretation with the expression 'qila' because it contradicts what the jurists have said about mentioning the disagreement between him and them, and stating that dripping according to him is not a condition absolutely, but it is not hidden that without this interpretation the saying of Abu Yusuf would contradict language and law; therefore they did not adopt it. His saying: 'a drop or two drops'; he only mentioned it with the letter of hesitation because of the differing expressions in mentioning the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The apparent meaning of most books is that flowing of one drop is sufficient, and from some is understood the requirement of two drops. His saying: 'And below the chin'; it is, with two fathas, the meeting place of the two bones which are the beds of the teeth. So in al-Qamus. It is conjoined to his saying: 'the ear'. It is outside the included [part] if by 'below' is meant what is under the chin, and inside it if by it is meant the other part of the chin.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage defines the boundaries of the face in wudu. The author states that the face includes the area below the chin. The lower chin is the meeting of the two jawbones where teeth grow. It completes the limits of the face from all four sides. The text also addresses a ruling about dripping: if a drop or two drops flow from a limb during wudu and are not wiped off, it does not invalidate wudu. The expression 'a drop or two drops' with hesitation reflects differing narrations from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: most books indicate one drop is sufficient, while some require two drops. Abu Yusuf's view that dripping is not a condition at all is rejected because it contradicts language and law. The commentary further explains that 'below the chin' can be interpreted either as under the chin (outside the face) or the lower part of the chin itself (inside the face).

Key Terms
أسفل الذقن (asfal al-dhaqan): The lower part of the chin; meeting place of the two jawbones.
قطرة أو قطرتان (qatra aw qatratan): A drop or two drops flowing from the limb during wudu.
التقاطر (al-taqattur): Dripping of water from a limb after washing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Dripping Drops: The Hanafi Ruling on Taqattur in Wudu
Ruling on dripping and intra-madhhab difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dripping during wudu and differentiate between the views of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Dripping one drop is sufficient for validity; Abu Yusuf's view is rejected.

When washing limbs in wudu, water often drips off. The Hanafi school has a specific condition called taqattur: if water does not drip, the limb may not be considered washed. This lesson explores the intra-madhhab disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say one drop suffices, some narrations require two, and Abu Yusuf says dripping is not a condition at all. The accepted view is one drop.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Understand that the passage discusses whether dripping (taqattur) is a condition for the validity of washing a limb in wudu.
The phrase 'أنه سال من العضو قطرة أو قطرتان' introduces the condition.
2
Recognize the three positions
Note the views: 1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: one drop sufficient. 2) Some narrations: two drops required. 3) Abu Yusuf: no condition.
The text says 'most books one drop, some two drops'.
3
Understand why Abu Yusuf's view is rejected
The commentary states that without the interpretation of 'qila', Abu Yusuf's view would contradict language and law; therefore it is not adopted.
The phrase 'مخالف للغة والشرع' is key.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority view (one drop) is the operative ruling for fatwa.
This is consistent with most Hanafi books.
