Dispute on Including Elbows and Ankles in Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inclusion of limits in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi majority includes elbows and ankles in wudu and evaluate Zufar's argument that they are not included.

Core Idea
A limit is included if it is of the same type as the thing limited—elbows and ankles are parts of the limbs.

This passage addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: Zufar held that the Qur'anic expression 'to the elbows' excludes the elbows themselves from washing. The author systematically refutes Zufar's proofs, affirming the majority position that the limits are included. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyze textual limits in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify that Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad hold that the elbows and ankles must be washed, and this is the mufta bihi.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Identify Zufar's opposing view and his initial proof
Zufar argues that the limit (ghayah) is not included in the limited (mughayya). State this as a universal rule.
The author points out the rule's falsity with counterexamples like 'to al-Masjid al-Aqsa'.
3
Analyze the author's critique of the 'conflict of semblances' argument
Zufar's second proof uses conflicting precedents: some limits are included (e.g., Quran memorization), some not (e.g., fasting until night). The author rejects this: if we know the type of limit (same kind as the limited), there is no doubt.
This is a critical refinement of the usul argument.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if one follows Zufar, washing elbows/ankles is sunnah; the majority holds it is fard, invalidating wudu if omitted.
Fatwa follows the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عَطَفَ على الوجه قوله : ( واليدين والرجلين مع المرفقين ، والكعبين) خلافاً لزفر ، فإنَّ عنده لا يَدْخُلُ المرفقان والكعبان في الغسل ؛ لأن الغاية لا تدخل تحت المغيَّا.
[1] قوله : لأن الغاية.. الخ ؛ اختار صاحب حل المشكلات»: «أن اللام على الغاية للعهد، فالمعنى أن هذه الغاية لا تدخل عنده»، وفيه خطأ ظاهر ؛ لأن الظاهر أن الشارح بصدد الاستدلال الزفر ، وعلى ما ذكره يكون الدعوى والدليل متحدين.
وحاصل الاستدلال أن المرافق والكعبين غاية للغسل، والغاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا. فإن قلت : إن أريد بالكبرى الكلية فبطلانها ظاهر ؛ لنقضه بقول : سُبْحَانَ الَّذِي أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلًا مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ إِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ الْأَقْصَصا ) (۱) ، وإن أريد الجزئية فلا تنتج في الشكل الأول.
قلت : المراد هو الكلية لكنها مقيّدة بما إذا لم يدل الدليل على خلافه، بأن يقال كل غاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا إلا إذا دلّ دليل على خلافه، هذا غاية التوجيه لاستدلاله. والمشهور في كتب الأصول في الاستدلال الزفرة لا اله الاستدلال بتعارض الأشباه ، وتقريره : أن أشباه هذه الغاية قد تعارضت فإن بعضها لا تدخل نحو : أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اليل ) ، وبعضها تدخل نحو : حفظت القرآن من أوله إلى آخره»، فوقع الشك في
(۲)
دخول هذه الغاية، والشك لا يثبت به شيء.
وهذا التقرير من الوجوه الفاسدة، فإنه يقال لا يخلو إما أن تكون تعلم أن هذه
الغاية من أي جنس أو لا تعلم.
فإن قلت : إنك لا تعلم فقد أقررت بجهلك ، ومَن عَلِمَ حجّة على من لم يعلم. وإن قلت : نعلم ، لم يبق الشك فبطل قولك بقولك. وقد يستدل له أيضاً بأن وجوب غسل ما قبل الكعب والمرفق متيقن، ودخول
المرفق والكعب مشكوك ، فالأخذ بالمتيقن وطرح المشكوك أولى.
والجواب عنه : أنا لا نُسلّم أنه مشكوك فيه، فإن الغاية التي تكون من جنس
صدر الكلام تدخل في المغيا، وهاهنا كذلك.
Then he conjoined to the face his saying: (and the hands and feet with the elbows and ankles) contrary to Zufar, for according to him the elbows and ankles are not included in the washing, because the limit is not included in the limited. His saying: 'because the limit... etc.'; the author of 'Hall al-Mushkilat' chose that the lam is for the known limit, so the meaning is that this limit is not included according to him, and there is an apparent error in it; because it is apparent that the commentator is in the position of proving the proof for Zufar, and according to what he mentioned the claim and proof become identical. The summary of the proof is that the elbows and ankles are the limit of washing, and the limit is not included in the limited. If you say: If by the major premise is meant the universal, then its falsity is apparent due to its refutation by His saying: 'Glorified is He who took His slave by night from al-Masjid al-Haram to al-Masjid al-Aqsa.' And if the particular is meant, then it does not produce a conclusion in the first figure. I say: What is meant is the universal, but it is restricted by when no evidence indicates otherwise, such that it is said: Every limit is not included in the limited unless evidence indicates otherwise. This is the best direction for his proof. And what is well-known in the books of usul in the proof of Zufar is the proof by conflict of semblances, and its explanation: that the semblances of this limit conflict, for some are not included, like: 'Complete the fasting until the night', and some are included, like: 'I memorized the Quran from its beginning to its end', so doubt occurred about the inclusion of this limit, and doubt does not prove anything. This explanation is among the corrupt aspects, for it is said: You either know that this limit is of which type or you do not know. If you say: You do not know, then you have admitted your ignorance, and one who knows is a proof against one who does not know. And if you say: We know, then doubt does not remain, so your statement is void by your statement. And it is also used as proof for him that the obligation of washing what is before the ankle and elbow is certain, and the inclusion of the elbow and ankle is doubtful, so taking the certain and leaving the doubtful is more appropriate. And the answer to it is: We do not accept that it is doubtful, for the limit that is of the same type as the beginning of the speech is included in the limited, and it is so here.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school: Zufar held that the elbows and ankles are not included in the washing of hands and feet because the Qur'anic expression 'to the elbows' and 'to the ankles' indicates a limit, and the limit is generally not included in what it limits (ghayah la tadkhul taht al-mughayya). The author critiques various defenses of Zufar's view. One attempted proof uses the universal rule that limits are not included, but this is refuted by counterexamples: 'from the Sacred Mosque to the Farthest Mosque' includes the latter; 'complete fasting until night' excludes night. To salvage the rule, it must be qualified: a limit is not included unless evidence shows it is of the same kind as the thing limited. The well-known usul argument for Zufar is based on conflicting precedents (ta'arud al-ashbah): some limits are included, some not, so doubt remains; doubt cannot establish obligation. But the author rejects this: if one knows the type of limit, there is no doubt. Another argument: the obligation of washing before the ankle/elbow is certain, inclusion is doubtful, so the certain (non-inclusion) should be taken. The answer: the limit here is of the same type as the limbs, so it is included. The Hanafi position (of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that elbows and ankles are included based on the Prophet's practice of pouring water over them.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghayah): The limit or end point mentioned in a text, e.g., 'to the elbows'.
المغيَّا (al-mughayya): The thing that has a limit; the part up to the limit.
تعارض الأشباه (ta'arud al-ashbah): Conflict of semblances or precedents, used as a method of argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, are the elbows and ankles included in the washing of hands and feet?
A) No, because the limit is never included.
B) Yes, because the limit is of the same type as the limbs.
C) No, because there is doubt.
D) Only if the water drips.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the elbows and ankles are included in the washing of hands and feet because the limit is of the same type as the limited limb.

Including the Ghayah Limit in Wudu: The Hanafi Principle
Proving elbow and ankle inclusion via ghayah principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle regarding the inclusion of the ghayah (limit) in the ruling and apply it to the washing of elbows and ankles in wudu.

Core Idea
A limit is included if it is of the same kind and covered by the wording.

In wudu, the Quran commands washing hands to the elbows and feet to the ankles. But are the elbows and ankles themselves washed? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi answer by analyzing the linguistic principle of ghayah. We will explore the dispute with Zufar and see how the Hanafi school resolves it using the criterion of tanawul (linguistic coverage).

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The legal question is whether the limit (ghayah) of a command—here, the elbows and ankles—is included in the obligation or excluded. The Quran says 'wash your hands to the elbows' (5:6); does 'to' include the elbows?
This is a usul al-fiqh problem with direct fiqh application.
2
State Zufar's position and his dalil
Zufar argued that the limit is generally not included, citing a universal principle. His evidence included examples like fasting until night where night is not part of the fast. He also used an argument from conflicting examples and doubt.
Understand his reasoning before seeing the refutation.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The commentator refutes Zufar by denying the universal principle. Instead, he asserts that inclusion depends on whether the limit is of the same kind as the limited and whether the preceding wording would encompass it. The elbows are of the same kind as the hands (parts of the limb) and the word 'hands' would include them if 'to' were omitted.
The key concept here is tanawul (linguistic coverage), not merely genus.
4
Examine the objections and answers
The passage lists five counterexamples (e.g., 'I fasted days from Saturday to Friday'—Friday not included despite being same kind). The Hanafi answer consistently distinguishes: in those examples, the wording does not tanawul the limit, unlike the wudu case where 'hands' naturally covers the elbow.
The commentator shows that each objection fails because the limit is not covered by the original wording.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the elbows and ankles are included in the washing obligation. Omission invalidates wudu. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and the majority, and it is preferred over Zufar's view.
Practical takeaway: ensure you wash the elbows and ankles thoroughly.

[1] قوله : لأن الغاية.. الخ ؛ اختار صاحب حل المشكلات»: «أن اللام على الغاية للعهد، فالمعنى أن هذه الغاية لا تدخل عنده»، وفيه خطأ ظاهر ؛ لأن الظاهر أن الشارح بصدد الاستدلال الزفر ، وعلى ما ذكره يكون الدعوى والدليل متحدين.
وحاصل الاستدلال أن المرافق والكعبين غاية للغسل، والغاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا. فإن قلت : إن أريد بالكبرى الكلية فبطلانها ظاهر ؛ لنقضه بقول : سُبْحَانَ الَّذِي أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلًا مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ إِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ الْأَقْصَصا ) (۱) ، وإن أريد الجزئية فلا تنتج في الشكل الأول.
قلت : المراد هو الكلية لكنها مقيّدة بما إذا لم يدل الدليل على خلافه، بأن يقال كل غاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا إلا إذا دلّ دليل على خلافه، هذا غاية التوجيه لاستدلاله. والمشهور في كتب الأصول في الاستدلال الزفرة لا اله الاستدلال بتعارض الأشباه ، وتقريره : أن أشباه هذه الغاية قد تعارضت فإن بعضها لا تدخل نحو : أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى اليل ) ، وبعضها تدخل نحو : حفظت القرآن من أوله إلى آخره»، فوقع الشك في
دخول هذه الغاية، والشك لا يثبت به شيء.
وهذا التقرير من الوجوه الفاسدة، فإنه يقال لا يخلو إما أن تكون تعلم أن هذه الغاية من أي جنس أو لا تعلم.
فإن قلت : إنك لا تعلم فقد أقررت بجهلك ، ومَن عَلِمَ حجّة على من لم يعلم. وإن قلت : نعلم ، لم يبق الشك فبطل قولك بقولك. وقد يستدل له أيضاً بأن وجوب غسل ما قبل الكعب والمرفق متيقن، ودخول المرفق والكعب مشكوك ، فالأخذ بالمتيقن وطرح المشكوك أولى.
والجواب عنه : أنا لا نُسلّم أنه مشكوك فيه، فإن الغاية التي تكون من جنس صدر الكلام تدخل في المغيا، وهاهنا كذلك.
[1] قوله : ونحن نقول ؛ الغرض منه إثبات المذهب المختار والردّ على وفر له الله ، بأن عدم دخول مطلق الغاية ممنوع ، بل الغايات إن كانت من جنس ما قبلها تدخل وإن لم تكن من جنسها لا تدخل.
[٢] قوله : يدخل تحت المغيا ؛ أورد على اعتبار التناول وعدمه للدخول وعدمه كما اختاره الشارح هاهنا بوجوه :
١. منها : أنه منقوض بقولهم: «صمت أياماً من السبت إلى الجمعة»، فإن الجمعة من جنس الأيام مع أنها لا تدخل تحت المغيا.
وأجيب . عنه :
بأن الجمعة وإن كانت من جنس الأيام لكن الأيام ليست متناولة لها كتناول اليد المرفق ، والعبرة إنما هو للتناول لا للجنسية. 
٢. ومنها : أنه منقوض بقولهم : بعت هذا البستان من هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط»، فإنه لا يدخل مع أنه من جنس المغيا.

والجواب عنه : إن الأيمان مبنيّة على عرف، والعرف قد يخالف مقتضى اللغة والوضع.
Because the limit (ghayah) is not included under the limited (mugayya). [1] His saying: 'Because the limit...' etc.; the author of 'Hall al-Mushkilat' chose: 'That the lam of the limit is for the covenant, so the meaning is that this limit is not included according to him,' and in it is a clear error; because the apparent is that the commentator is in the process of inferring with Zufar's method, and according to what he mentioned, the claim and the proof would be identical. The summary of the inference is that the forearms and the ankles are the limit for washing, and the limit is not included under the limited. If you say: If what is meant by the major premise is universal, then its falsehood is apparent; because it is contradicted by the saying: 'Exalted is He who took His Servant by night from al-Masjid al-Haram to al-Masjid al-Aqsa' (Quran 17:1), and if what is meant is particular, then it does not produce a result in the first figure. I say: What is meant is the universal, but it is restricted by the condition that there is no evidence to the contrary; i.e., it is said: Every limit is not included under the limited unless evidence indicates otherwise. This is the fullest orientation of his inference. And what is well-known in the books of Usul in the inference of Zufar is the inference by conflicting examples (ta'arud al-ashbah), and its explanation: That the examples of this limit conflict; some are not included, like: 'Complete the fast until the night' (Quran 2:187), and some are included, like: 'I memorized the Qur'an from its beginning to its end,' so doubt arose about the inclusion of this limit, and doubt establishes nothing. This explanation is among the corrupt aspects, for it is said: It is not free from either you know that this limit is of which kind or you do not. If you say: You do not know, then you have admitted your ignorance, and one who knows is a proof against one who does not. If you say: We know, then doubt does not remain, so your statement is invalidated by your statement. And it may also be inferred for it that the obligation of washing that which is before the ankle and elbow is certain, and the inclusion of the ankle and elbow is doubtful, so taking the certain and discarding the doubtful is more appropriate. The answer to it: We do not accept that it is doubtful; for the limit that is of the same kind as the beginning of the statement is included in the limited, and such is the case here. [1] His saying: 'And we say'; the purpose of it is to prove the chosen madhhab and to refute Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him, by denying that the absolute non-inclusion of the limit is forbidden; rather, the limits if they are of the same kind as what precedes them, they are included, and if they are not of the same kind, they are not included. [2] His saying: 'enters under the limited'; objections were raised against the consideration of inclusion or non-inclusion as chosen by the commentator here in several ways: 1. Among them: It is contradicted by their saying: 'I fasted days from Saturday to Friday,' for Friday is of the same kind as the days yet it is not included under the limited. And it was answered about it: That Friday, although it is of the same kind as the days, yet the days do not include it like the inclusion of the hand by the forearm; the consideration is only for inclusion, not for the kind. 2. Among them: It is contradicted by their saying: 'I sold this garden from this wall to this wall,' for it is not included even though it is of the same kind as the limited. 3. Among them: It is contradicted by their saying: 'You are divorced from one to three,' for the limit is of the same kind as the limited yet it is not included; rather, two occur. 4. Among them: It is contradicted by their saying: 'He has dirhams on me from one to ten,' for this acknowledgement necessitates nine according to Abu Hanifah, and the answer to them is similar to what has passed. 5. Among them: It is contradicted by the case of one who swears 'I will not speak to so-and-so until tomorrow,' for tomorrow is not included even though if he had not mentioned the words 'until tomorrow' and sufficed with his saying 'I will not speak to so-and-so,' it would have encompassed the entire future. The answer to it: Oaths are based on custom, and custom may contradict the requirements of language and usage.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed fiqh commentary on the ruling regarding the inclusion of the limit (ghayah) in wudu—specifically whether the elbows and ankles are washed. The author begins by explaining Zufar's inference that the limit is not included, then refutes it. He presents multiple objections and answers, ultimately establishing that the limit is included if it is of the same kind as the limited and is covered by the wording. The commentary refines the principle by distinguishing between the nature of the limit and whether the preceding wording would encompass it without the limit phrase. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the elbows and ankles are included in the washing obligation, and this is supported by linguistic reasoning.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The limit or endpoint of an action or command, indicated by words like 'ila' (to/until).
المغيا (al-mughayyā): The thing that is limited or ended by the ghayah; the object of the command or action.
التناول (al-tanāwul): Linguistic coverage; whether the words of the command would encompass the limit if the limit phrase were omitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position established in this passage, are the elbows and ankles included in the wudu washing obligation?
A) No, because the limit is always excluded.
B) Yes, because they are of the same kind as the hands and feet and are covered by the wording.
C) No, because there is doubt about their inclusion.
D) Yes, but only according to Zufar's view.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the limit (ghayah) of a command is included in the ruling if it is of the same kind and would be covered by the wording without the limit phrase.

The Hanafi Rule of Ghayah: Inclusion Depends on Linguistic Incorporation (Tanāwul)
Hanafi principle of ghayah with objections and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ghayah to determine whether an endpoint expressed by 'ila' is included in the ruling, using the criterion of linguistic inclusion (tanāwul).

Core Idea
An endpoint is included only if the preceding term would encompass it without 'ila'.

In Hanafi fiqh, the word 'ila' (to, until) does not automatically include or exclude the endpoint. The key is whether the initial part of the utterance (ṣadr al-kalām) naturally encompasses that endpoint. This lesson unpacks that rule using examples from wudu, fasting, oaths, and sale contracts, and addresses common objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ṣadr al-kalām and the ghayah
Determine the term before 'ila' (the ruled-upon range) and the term after it (the endpoint). For example, in 'wash your hands to the elbows', the ṣadr is 'hands' and the ghayah is 'elbows'.
2
Evaluate linguistic inclusion (tanāwul)
Ask: Without 'ila', would the ṣadr al-kalām normally include the ghayah in common Arabic usage? Check if the term is used to encompass that specific object in everyday speech.
Hanafis prioritize tanāwul over mere similarity of kind (jins).
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
If tanāwul exists, the ghayah is included in the ruling; if not, it is excluded. The wudu example (hand includes elbow) results in inclusion, while the fasting example (fast does not include night) results in exclusion.
4
Consider exceptions for oaths
In oaths and similar contexts, custom (urf) can override the linguistic rule. For instance, 'I will not speak to you until tomorrow' excludes tomorrow because custom treats it as the end of the period.
The passage explicitly states: 'Oaths are based on custom.'

Source Text

ونحن نقول: إن كانت الغاية بحيث لو لم تدخل فيها كلمة : إلى، لم يتناولها صدر الكلام، لم يدخل تحت المغيا، كالليل في الصوم. وإن كانت بحيث يتناولها الصدرُ كالمتنازع فيه تدخل تحت المغيا

[٢] قوله : يدخل تحت المغيا؛ أورد على اعتبار التناول وعدمه للدخول وعدمه كما اختاره الشارح هاهنا بوجوه :

والجواب عنه : إن الأيمان مبنيّة على عرف، والعرف قد يخالف مقتضى اللغة والوضع.
[۱] بناء على أن للنحويين في : إلى ؛ أربعة مذاهب:
[۲] الأول : دخولُ ما بعدها فيما قبلها
٦. ومنها : إنه منقوض بقوله : سُبْحَنَ الَّذِي أَسْرَى بِعَبْدِهِ لَيْلًا مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ الْحَرَامِ إِلَى الْمَسْجِدِ الْأَقْصَا، فإنّ الغاية ليست من جنس المغيًا مع أنها داخلة. والجواب عنه : إنا لا نسلّم دخولها في الآية، ولا ضير في ذلك، فإنّ دخوله في المسجد الأقصى ثبت بالأحاديث المشهورة، وأما في حل المشكلات»: «أنا لا نسلّم أن المسجد الأقصى داخل في السير؛ لأنه إنما يكون داخلاً لو دخل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من جانب وخرج من جانب آخر، وليس كذلك، بل دخله وذهب إلى الصخرة، ثم صعد إلى ما شاء الله، كما عُرِف في قصة المعراج». انتهى.
ليس بشيء، فإنَّ اعتبار الدخول من جانب والخروج من جانب آخر في مفهوم السير مما لا دليل عليه، علا أن خروجه لـه من المسجد الأقصى ثابت في رواية «صحيح مسلم، وغيره.
[١]قوله : بناء؛ نصب على أنه مفعول له؛ لقوله : نقول أو على أنه مفعول له؛ لقوله : لم تدخل وتدخل، أو على أنه مفعول مطلق؛ أي بني ذلك التفصيلُ بناءً على أنَّ للنحويين في «إلى»: أي في دخول ما بعدها في ما قبلها، وعدم دخوله أربعة مذهب. [٢] قوله : الأول؛ إنّما قدمه مع كونه مذهباً للنحويين ضعيفاً؛ لكونه وجودياً، والوجودي أشرف من العدمي، وقدمهما على الاشتراك؛ لكون الحقيقة والمجاز راجحاً على الاشتراك، وقدمها على الرابع؛ لكونه مشتملاً على التفصيل، فهو أليقُ بالتأخير. [٣]قوله : فيما قبلها؛ المضاف محذوف؛ أي في حكم ما قبلها، هذا إذا أريد بما قبلها المغيا، وإن أريد به الحكم السابق فلا حاجة إلى حذفه.
And we say: If the endpoint is such that, had the word 'ila' not been mentioned, the preceding part of the speech would not have included it, then it does not fall under the ruled-upon [range], like the night in fasting. And if it is such that the preceding part does include it, as in the contested case, then it does fall under the ruled-upon [range]. [1] His statement: 'And we say' – the purpose of it is to establish the chosen school and to refute the one who differentiates (may Allah grant him success), by [stating] that the absolute non-inclusion of the endpoint is not accepted. Rather, endpoints: if they are of the same kind as what precedes them, they enter; if not, they do not enter. [2] His statement: 'falls under the ruled-upon' – objections have been raised against using [linguistic] inclusion or non-inclusion as the criterion for entrance or non-entrance, as the commentator has chosen here, in several ways: 1. Among them: It is invalidated by their saying: 'I fasted days from Saturday to Friday' – for Friday is of the same kind as the days yet it does not fall under the ruled-upon. And it is answered about it: that Friday, although of the same kind as the days, the word 'days' does not encompass it as the word 'hand' encompasses the elbow; the consideration is only for [linguistic] inclusion, not for kind. 2. Among them: It is invalidated by their saying: 'I sold this garden from this wall to this wall' – for it [the second wall] does not enter even though it is of the same kind as the ruled-upon. 3. Among them: It is invalidated by their saying: 'You are divorced from one to three' – for the endpoint is of the same kind as the ruled-upon yet it does not enter; rather, two [divorces] occur. 4. Among them: It is invalidated by their saying: 'He owes me dirhams from one to ten' – for this acknowledgment necessitates nine dirhams according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). And the answer to them is similar to what preceded. 5. Among them: It is invalidated by the case where someone swears: 'I will not speak to so-and-so until tomorrow' – for tomorrow does not enter, even though if he had not said 'until tomorrow' and sufficed with his statement 'I will not speak to so-and-so', it would encompass all the future. And the answer about it is: Oaths are based on custom, and custom may contradict the dictates of language and linguistic convention. [1] This is based on the fact that grammarians have four schools regarding 'ila': [2] The first: inclusion of what comes after it in what precedes it. 6. Among them: It is invalidated by His saying: 'Exalted is He who took His servant by night from al-Masjid al-Haram to al-Masjid al-Aqsa' – for the endpoint is not of the same kind as the ruled-upon yet it is included. And the answer about it: We do not concede its inclusion in the verse, and there is no harm in that, for his entry into al-Masjid al-Aqsa is established by well-known hadiths. As for [the book] 'Hall al-Mushkilat': 'We do not concede that al-Masjid al-Aqsa is included in the journey, for it would only be included if the Prophet (peace be upon him) had entered from one side and exited from another, and this is not the case. Rather, he entered it and went to the Rock, then ascended to whatever Allah willed, as is known in the story of the Mi'raj.' This is nothing, for considering entering from one side and exiting from another in the concept of journey has no evidence; moreover, his exit (peace be upon him) from al-Masjid al-Aqsa is established in a narration of Sahih Muslim and others. [1] His statement: 'based' – it is in the accusative as an object of purpose for his statement 'we say', or as an object of purpose for his statement 'does not enter and enters', or as an absolute object; i.e., that detailed classification was built on the fact that grammarians have four schools regarding 'ila', that is, regarding the inclusion or non-inclusion of what comes after it in what precedes it. [2] His statement: 'the first' – he mentioned it first even though it is a weak school among grammarians, because it is affirmative, and the affirmative is nobler than the negative; and he gave it precedence over [the school of] common usage because the real and metaphorical are preponderant over common usage; and he gave it precedence over the fourth because it includes detail, so it is more fitting to be delayed. [3] His statement: 'in what precedes it' – the genitive construct is omitted; i.e., 'in the ruling of what precedes it'. This if by 'what precedes it' the ruled-upon is meant, but if the previous ruling is meant, then there is no need for omission.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah (or Al-Hidayah) discussing the linguistic principle of ghayah (endpoint) when expressed by the word 'ila' (to, until). The issue directly impacts fiqh rulings such as the limits of washing in wudu (hands to elbows) or the extent of fasting (dawn to sunset). The author presents the Hanafi position that inclusion of the endpoint depends on whether the initial wording would naturally encompass it without 'ila'. For example, in 'wash your hands to the elbows,' the word 'hands' naturally includes the elbows, so the elbows are included (the Hanafi view). In 'fast from dawn to night,' the word 'fast' does not naturally include night, so night is not included (the fast ends at sunset). The text then lists several objections (naqd) from critics who argue that this criterion fails in certain examples like 'I fasted from Saturday to Friday' (Friday is not included even though it is a day) or 'I sold this garden from this wall to that wall' (the second wall is excluded despite being part of the garden). The author responds to each: in the fasting example, the word 'days' does not specifically include Friday; in the sale example, the walls are not part of the sold property. For oaths ('I will not speak to you until tomorrow'), custom (urf) overrides linguistic generality. The passage also notes that grammarians have four schools on the inclusion of 'ila'—the first being inclusion of what follows in what precedes. A final objection from the Quranic verse about the Night Journey is answered by distinguishing the verse's explicit wording from the actual event (the Prophet entered and exited al-Aqsa, which is not simply 'traveling to'). The author rejects that distinction as unsupported. The commentary concludes with grammatical analysis of the word 'bina' (based) and why the first school is mentioned first despite its weakness.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The endpoint or limit expressed by 'ila' (to/until) in a legal or linguistic statement
المغيا (al-mughayyā): The range or ruling that is delimited by the ghayah; the part before 'ila'
تناول (tanāwul): Linguistic inclusion: whether a term naturally encompasses another object/meaning
صدر الكلام (ṣadr al-kalām): The initial part of the utterance (the subject or predicate) that, without 'ila', would or would not include the endpoint
جنس (jins): Kind or category; similarity in genus between the ghayah and the mughayya
عرف (urf): Custom or common usage that may override literal linguistic meaning, especially in oaths

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this passage, when does the endpoint (ghayah) expressed by 'ila' fall under the ruling of the preceding term?
A) Always, regardless of context.
B) Never, the endpoint is always excluded.
C) Only when the preceding term would naturally encompass the endpoint without 'ila'.
D) Only when the endpoint is of the same kind as the preceding term.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for ghayah: inclusion depends on whether the initial term (ṣadr al-kalām) would naturally include the endpoint without 'ila'.

The Meaning of 'Ila' in Wudu: Four Grammatical Schools
Grammatical and legal analysis of the particle 'ila' in the wudu verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the inclusion of elbows in wudu and explain how the grammatical interpretation of 'ila' supports it.

Core Idea
The inclusion of elbows in wudu depends on the genus of the object, not on any automatic implication of the preposition 'ila'.

The wudu verse commands washing 'your hands to the elbows' (ila al-marafiq). How do we know the elbows are included? The answer lies in a sophisticated grammatical-legal analysis of the particle 'ila'. In this lesson, we explore the four grammatical schools of thought on 'ila' and see how the Hanafi school adopts the fourth: inclusion when the object is of the same genus. This resolves the fiqh ruling that washing the elbows is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Does 'ila' in the wudu verse include the elbows or stop at them? The answer determines whether washing elbows is obligatory.
The verse: 'wash your faces and your hands to the elbows' (Quran 5:6).
2
Survey the four grammatical schools
First: 'ila' always includes except metaphorically. Second: never includes except metaphorically. Third: ambiguous (ishtirak). Fourth: inclusion depends on genus.
The fourth school is attributed to Ibn al-Humam.
3
Evaluate each school's implication for fiqh
Only the fourth school directly supports the Hanafi ruling: since elbows are of the same genus as hands, they are included.
The third school would require external evidence; the first two are either too absolute or too restrictive.
4
Confirm the Hanafi fiqh position
The Hanafi school holds that washing the elbows is wajib (obligatory) based on the fourth school and confirmed by Prophetic practice.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi madhhab.

Source Text

إلا مجازا".

والثاني: عدم الدخول إلا مجازاً.

والثالث : الاشتراك".

(1) قوله : إلا مجازاً ؛ أورد عليه أن الأصل في الاستثناء أن يكون المستثنى من جنس المستثنى منه ، وهاهنا ليس كذلك.

وأجيب عنه : بأن معنى كلامه هكذا دخول ما بعدها فيما قبلها في جميع الأوقات إلا وقت المجاز، وهو ما إذا وجدت قرينة تمنع عن الدخول، فحينئذ لا يدخل. [2] قوله : والثاني ؛ هذا هو مذهب أكثر النحاة، كما ذكره الرضي في «شرح الكافية»، وصححه ابن هشام في مغني اللبيب [3]قوله : والثالث : الاشتراك ؛ الظاهر من كلامه هاهنا وكلامه في «التوضيح) أنَّ المراد به الاشتراك اللفظي وحمله على الاشتراك المعنوي، كما صدر من الفاضل عبد الله اللبيب بن عبد الحكيم اللاهوري في حواشيه على التلويح يأبى عنه السباق والسياق، وحينئذ يرد عليه : أولاً : أن مذهب الاشتراك اللفظي غير معروف فيما بين النحويين، وهو أنها لا تدل على الدخول ولا على عدمه، بل كل منهما محمول على قرينة خارجيّة، وهو المختار على ما ذكره سعد الدين التفتازاني في «التلويح )، فمع ضمه تصير المذاهب خمسة، ولم يقل به أحد. فإن قلت : إن المذهب المختار هو المذهب الرابع ؛ إذ حاصله أنّ إلى لا تدل على الدخول ولا على عدمه، بل كلّ منهما يدور مع الدليل، غايته أنه اعتبر الدليل من اللفظ ، وهو تناولُ الصدور وعدمه. قلت : المذهب المختار مذكور في كتبهم مع المذهب الرابع مغايراً له، فكيف يكون عينه، وأيضاً المتمذهب بتلك المذاهب لم يحصر دليل الدخول وعدمه في التناول وعدمه، بل هو عنده أعم منه ، فكيف يكون هو هو ؟

فإن قلت : لعل الشارح اطلع على قول بعض النحاة بالاشتراك اللفظي.

قلت: هب، وإن كان هذا محتملا ، لكن خلوّ كتب النحاة المعتبرة عن ذكره أول دليل على عدم اعتباره وبالجملة فكان ينبغي له أن لا يذكر الاشتراك، ويذكر مذهب التحويل.

[1] قوله : والرابع ؛ اختار ابن الهمام مؤلّف «فتح القدير» في «تحرير الأصول» أن إلى لا تدل على الدخول ولا عدمه، والكل يدور مع الدليل، وردّ هذا المذهب الرابع بأنه لا يلزم من الجنسية الدخول ، و من عدمها عدمه إلا أن يثبت استقراء الدخول عند الجنسية وعدمه عند عدمها ، فيحمل عليه حملاً على الأعم الأغلب.

والرابع: الدخولُ إن كانَ ما بعدها من جنس ما قبلها، وعدمه إن لم يكن. فهذا المذهبُ الرَّابع يوافق ما ذكرنا في الليل والمرافق.
The Book of Purity: Wudu. Except metaphorically. The second: non-inclusion except metaphorically. The third: ambiguity. (1) His saying: 'except metaphorically'; it was objected that the principle in exception is that the excepted item is of the same genus as the excepted-from, and here it is not so. And it was answered: the meaning of his words is that what comes after it enters into what comes before it in all times except the time of metaphor, which is when a contextual indicator (qarina) prevents inclusion, then it does not enter. [2] His saying: 'the second'; this is the school of most grammarians, as al-Radi mentioned in his Sharh al-Kafiya, and Ibn Hisham validated it in Mughni al-Labib. [3] His saying: 'the third: ambiguity'; the apparent from his words here and in al-Tawdih is that what is meant is verbal homonymy (ishtirak lafzi), and its interpretation as semantic homonymy (ishtirak ma'nawi) as issued from al-Fadil 'Abd Allah al-Labib ibn 'Abd al-Hakim al-Lahori in his glosses on al-Talwih is rejected by the context and the flow. And then it is objected: first, that the school of verbal homonymy is not known among grammarians, and that it does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion; rather each is carried on an external contextual indicator, and this is the chosen view according to what Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani mentioned in al-Talwih. So with its addition the schools become five, and no one has held that. If you say: the chosen school is the fourth school, since its gist is that 'ila' does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion, rather each revolves with the evidence, except that he considered the evidence from the wording, namely the taking in of the chests and its absence. I say: the chosen school is mentioned in their books distinct from the fourth school, so how could it be the same? Also, the one who follows those schools did not confine the evidence for inclusion and non-inclusion to taking in and its absence, rather it is more general than that, so how could it be identical? If you say: perhaps the commentator came across the statement of some grammarians regarding verbal homonymy. I say: granted, even if this is possible, but the absence of its mention in reliable grammar books is the first proof of its non-consideration. In summary, he should not have mentioned homonymy and should have mentioned the school of transformation (tahwil). [1] His saying: 'the fourth'; Ibn al-Humam, author of Fath al-Qadir, chose in Tahrir al-Usul that 'ila' does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion, and everything revolves with evidence. And this fourth school was rejected because inclusion does not necessarily follow from genus-ness, nor non-inclusion from lack of it, unless induction establishes inclusion when there is genus-ness and non-inclusion when there is not, so it is interpreted according to the more general and predominant. [2] His saying: 'this school... etc.'; meaning, once you understand this detail, we say: the fourth school agrees with what we mentioned, for night, since it is not of the same genus as what precedes it, does not enter under the limit. And the fourth: inclusion if what comes after it is of the same genus as what comes before it, and non-inclusion if it is not. So this fourth school agrees with what we mentioned regarding night and elbows.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and legal analysis of the particle 'ila' (to/until) in the context of the wudu verse (Quran 5:6: 'wash your faces and your hands to the elbows'). The commentator presents four schools (madhahib) of grammarians regarding whether 'ila' implies inclusion of its object (the elbows) or not. The first school (not explicitly named) holds that 'ila' always implies inclusion except metaphorically. The second school holds that it never implies inclusion except metaphorically. The third school holds that 'ila' is ambiguous (ishtirak), neither indicating inclusion nor exclusion, but relying on external evidence. The fourth school, attributed to Ibn al-Humam, holds that inclusion depends on whether the object (the word after 'ila') is of the same genus as the preceding word. The commentator refines these views, noting that the chosen opinion (al-mukhtar) of the later scholars like al-Taftazani is that 'ila' itself is neutral and the ruling is determined by contextual indicators. Importantly, the commentator reconciles this with the Hanafi fiqh position: the elbows (al-marafiq) are part of the hand, thus of the same genus, and are included in the washing. Conversely, 'night' (al-layl) in other contexts is not of the same genus and would not be included. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling that washing the elbows is obligatory (wajib) is supported by this linguistic analysis.

Key Terms
الدخول (al-dukhul): Inclusion of the object of 'ila' in the ruling of the preceding verb.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage where 'ila' does not indicate inclusion despite literal form.
الاشتراك (al-ishtirak): Ambiguity: 'ila' carries both meanings equally, requiring external evidence.
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator (verbal or situational) that determines the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, why are the elbows included in the washing of the hands in wudu?
A) Because the word 'ila' always implies inclusion.
B) Because the elbows are of the same genus as the hands (the object of washing).
C) Because there is an external textual evidence ordering inclusion.
D) Because the fourth grammatical school mandates inclusion in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on washing the elbows in wudu is based on the fourth grammatical interpretation of 'ila': inclusion when the object is of the same genus, confirmed by prophetic practice.

The Four Madhhabs on the Meaning of 'إلى'
Four madhhabs on 'إلى' with tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four madhhabs on 'إلى', explain why the Hanafi school prefers the fourth, and apply it to the examples of night in fasting and elbows in wudu.

Core Idea
إلى does not inherently include or exclude; context determines, preserving certainty.

The Arabic preposition 'إلى' (to/until) is used in key Quranic verses such as 'ثم أتموا الصيام إلى الليل' (complete the fast until night). Does it include the endpoint (night) or not? Scholars debated this across four madhhabs. This lesson unpacks those positions, the Hanafi preferred view, and its practical impact on fasting and wudu rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four madhhabs on 'إلى'
List the four positions: (1) always includes endpoint except metaphorically, (2) never includes except metaphorically, (3) homonymous (can mean both), (4) context-dependent via external evidence (jins).
Focus on the fourth as the Hanafi relied-upon view.
2
Understand the tarjih for the fourth madhhab
The first two cancel each other; homonymy creates doubt. The fourth uses external clues (qarina) to determine inclusion, preserving prior certainty (istiṣḥāb).
The chosen view (mukhtār) is from Taftazani and Ibn al-Humam.
3
Apply to fasting: 'until night'
Night is not of the same kind (jins) as the day of fasting, so 'إلى' does not include it. Fasting continues until actual nightfall (certainty).
This rejects the view that the fast ends at sunset if 'إلى' included night.
4
Apply to wudu: 'wash your hands to the elbows'
Elbows are of the same kind as the forearm, so 'إلى' includes them. Washing must include the elbows.
This matches the Hanafi ruling that includes elbows in the wash.
5
Compare with other schools' practical outcomes
If a Shafi'i held that 'إلى' always includes, they would include the night in the fast (invalid). Understanding the dispute clarifies the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
The Hanafi position avoids doubt and adheres to linguistic induction.

[1] قوله : وأما الثلاثة ؛ دفع دخل مقدّر تقريره أنه لما لم يعمل بالمذاهب الباقية، وحاصل الدفع أنه إنّما اخترنا المذهب الرابع ؛ لأنَّ العمل به نتيجة المذاهب الثلاثة ؛ لأنّ الأوليين وهو الدخول إلا مجازاً، وعدم الدخول إلا مجازاً متعارضان ظاهراً ، فتساويا فأوجبا بتساويهما الشك في مواضع دخول : «إلى».
وكذلك الاشتراك أوجب الشك في الدخول وعدمه، ففيما إذا كان صدر الكلام غير متناول مثل : أَتِمُّوا الصِّيَامَ إِلَى الَّيْلِ ) ، عدم الدخول معلوم ؛ لعدم التجانس، فوقع الشك بدخول «إلى» هل هو مستعمل في الدخول أم في عدم الدخول ؟ فلا يثبت الدخول بالشك ؛ لأنَّ اليقين السابق لا يرتفع بمجرد الشك، وفيما إذا كان متناولاً الشك لما الدخول معلوم للتجانس، وبدخول «إلى» وقعَ الشك في الخروج ، فلا يخرج ا
وبهذا يظهر أن المراد من قوله : فتساويا، ليس ما ذكره أخي جلبي في «ذخيرة العقبي» بقوله : «والتساوي يوجبُ السقوط، فإن المجتهد كيف يعمل بأحد المتساويين
And the fourth: inclusion if what follows is of the same kind as what precedes it, and non-inclusion if it is not. So this fourth madhhab agrees with what we mentioned regarding night and elbows. It indicates neither inclusion nor non-inclusion, rather each is based on an external contextual clue, and it is the chosen view according to what Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani mentioned in 'al-Talwih', so with its addition the madhhabs become five, and no one held it. If you say: The chosen madhhab is the fourth; since its essence is that 'ila' does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion, rather each revolves with the evidence, the only difference being that he considered the evidence from the wording, which is the inclusion or non-inclusion of the beginning of the statement. I say: The chosen madhhab is mentioned in their books alongside the fourth, different from it, so how could it be identical? Also, the one who follows those madhhabs did not restrict the evidence for inclusion and non-inclusion to the inclusion or non-inclusion of the beginning, rather it is more general in his view, so how could they be the same? If you say: Perhaps the commentator came across the statement of some grammarians regarding homonymy. I say: Granted, even if this is probable, but the absence of its mention in the reliable books of grammar is the first proof of its non-consideration. In summary, he should not have mentioned homonymy, and should have mentioned the madhhab of conversion. [1] His statement: And the fourth; Ibn al-Humam, author of 'Fath al-Qadir', in 'Tahrir al-Usul' chose that 'ila' does not indicate inclusion nor non-inclusion, and all revolves with the evidence. This fourth madhhab was refuted by saying that inclusion does not necessarily follow from similarity of kind, nor non-inclusion from its absence, unless an inductive survey establishes inclusion when there is similarity and non-inclusion when there is not, then it is applied based on the more common and prevalent. [2] His statement: So this madhhab... etc.; meaning, once you understand this detail, we say: The fourth madhhab agrees with what we mentioned, for when night is not of the same kind as what precedes it, it does not enter under the end limit. As for the first three: the first is opposed by the second, so they balanced each other, and the third also necessitated equality, so doubt occurs in places where the word 'ila' is used. So in the example of night in fasting, doubt occurred about inclusion and non-inclusion, so inclusion is not established by doubt. And the elbows, since they are of the same kind as what precedes them, are included therein. There is a discussion: We do not concede that the fourth agrees with what he previously mentioned because the basis of the fourth madhhab is similarity and non-similarity, while the basis of what he mentioned is the inclusion or non-inclusion of the beginning of the statement, and there is a difference between them. Do you not see that Friday enters into his statement 'I fasted from Saturday to Friday' according to the fourth madhhab due to similarity, but does not enter according to what he previously mentioned due to the absence of inclusion? It was answered: His purpose is that what the grammarians mean by similarity and its absence is inclusion and its absence, and then there is no doubt about their agreement. [1] His statement: And as for the three; it rebuts a presumed objection whose summary is: Since he did not act on the remaining madhhabs, and the gist of the rebuttal is that we only chose the fourth madhhab because acting on it is the result of the three madhhabs; because the first two, which are inclusion except metaphorically and non-inclusion except metaphorically, are apparently contradictory, so they balanced each other, and their balance necessitated doubt in places of 'ila's entry. Similarly, homonymy necessitated doubt about inclusion and non-inclusion. So when the beginning of the statement does not include, like 'complete the fast until night', non-inclusion is known due to lack of similarity, so doubt arises about 'ila': is it used for inclusion or non-inclusion? So inclusion is not established by doubt because prior certainty is not removed by mere doubt. And when it does include, inclusion is known due to similarity, and with 'ila's entry doubt arises about exclusion, so it is not excluded. And by this it becomes clear that what is meant by his statement 'so they balanced' is not what Akhi Jilabi mentioned in 'Dhakirat al-Uqba' by his statement: 'Equality necessitates dropping; for how can a mujtahid act with one of two equals?'
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the meaning of the Arabic preposition 'إلى' (to/until) and whether it implies inclusion of the endpoint or not. The context is its application in fiqh, particularly in verses like 'ثم أتموا الصيام إلى الليل' (complete the fast until night). The author discusses four madhhabs (schools of thought) on this linguistic-legal issue. The first three are: (1) 'إلى' always implies inclusion except metaphorically, (2) it never implies inclusion except metaphorically, and (3) it is homonymous (indicates both, depending on usage). The fourth madhhab, attributed to Ibn al-Humam and others, holds that 'إلى' does not inherently indicate either; rather, the ruling is determined by external context. The author notes that the chosen view (al-mukhtar) according to Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani is that 'إلى' indicates neither, and when added, there would be five madhhabs, but no one held that view. There is clarification that the fourth madhhab agrees with what was mentioned earlier about the examples of night and elbows: night does not enter because it is not of the same kind as the preceding (fasting), while elbows do enter because they are of the same kind as the forearms (in wudu). The discussion includes a critique that the fourth madhhab bases inclusion on similarity of kind, while the earlier position was based on the linguistic scope (tawin) of the initial part of the statement. The response reconciles them by stating that grammarians intend 'similarity' to mean 'inclusion in the scope'. The author then explains why the fourth madhhab is chosen: because the first two madhhabs (pure inclusion and pure non-inclusion) cancel each other out, and homonymy also creates doubt, so the safest is to rely on external evidence. This leads to the fiqh principle that prior certainty (e.g., that the night is not included in the fast) is not removed by doubt, so the fast continues until certainty of nightfall. The passage concludes with a clarification about the meaning of 'balance' in the context of mujtahid's preference.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): The Arabic preposition meaning 'to' or 'until', whose inclusion of the endpoint is debated in fiqh and usul.
الدخول (al-dakhul): Inclusion; whether the object of 'إلى' is included in the ruling of the preceding verb.
الاشتراك اللفظي (al-ishtirak al-lafzi): Homonymy; the same word having multiple meanings, implying 'إلى' could mean either inclusion or exclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen (mufta bihi) Hanafi view, why is the night not included in the fasting verse 'أتموا الصيام إلى الليل'?
A) Because 'إلى' never includes the endpoint.
B) Because 'إلى' is homonymous and here means exclusion.
C) Because night is not of the same kind as the day of fasting.
D) Because the verb 'أتموا' (complete) implies exclusion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon view is that 'إلى' does not inherently indicate inclusion or exclusion; the ruling depends on external evidence, primarily the similarity of kind between the starting point and the endpoint.

Including the Elbows and Ankles in Wudu: The Ghayat al-Isqat Argument
Intra-Hanafi dispute over including the limit in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two formulations of the 'ghayat al-isqat' argument and identify how they prove that the elbows and ankles are included in wudu, refuting Zufar's position.

Core Idea
The elbows and ankles are washed because 'ila' marks the limit of removal, not the limit of washing.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute over the interpretation of the preposition 'ila' in the wudu verse. While Zufar argued that 'ila' excludes the limit (elbows/ankles) from washing, the majority of Hanafi imams including Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad held that the limit is included. The key argument, known as 'ghayat al-isqat' (limit of removal), is presented in two formulations. Understanding this reasoning deepens your appreciation of Hanafi usul al-fiqh and its application to daily worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the issue is whether the elbows and ankles are included in the washing for wudu. The majority says yes, Zufar says no.
The difference stems from interpreting 'ila'.
2
State Zufar's argument
Zufar holds that 'ila' indicates the end of a limit, so what comes after the limit is excluded. Thus, washing stops before the elbows and ankles.
3
Explain the first formulation of ghayat al-isqat
Reinterpret the syntax: 'ila' attaches to 'removal' (isqat) not to 'washing'. The estimation: 'Wash your hands, removing the washing up to the elbows.' Thus, the limit exits from removal, so it enters the washing.
This clever inversion answers Zufar on his own terms.
4
Explain the second formulation
If the initial part already encompasses the limit (e.g., hand includes elbow), then mentioning the limit excludes what is beyond. But since the hand does not naturally include the elbow, the limit is mentioned to extend the ruling, so the elbow is included.
This formulation is considered more precise by some.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
The majority view is the mufta bihi position. The practical consequence is that the elbows and ankles must be washed; failure invalidates wudu.

مختاراً في حتى لا يستلزم كونه مختاراً في إلى فإن بينهما فرقاً من وجه ولذا قال الرضي الأظهر دخول ما بعد حتى في حكم ما قبلها بخلاف إلى فإن الأظهر فيها عدم الدخول إلا مع القرينة. انتهى. قوله : وما ذكروا ... الخ المذكور المشهور في كتب الأصوليين لإثبات دخول المرافق والكعبين رداً على زفر أن الغاية في قوله إلى المرافق وقوله إلى الكعبين غاية الإسقاط وله تقريران: التقرير الأول أن إلى في القول المذكور ليست متعلقة بالغسل حتى تكون غاية له ويخرج ما بعدها عما قبلها بناءً على أن الغاية لا تدخل تحت حكم المغيا بل هي غاية للإسقاط ومتعلقة به والتقدير اغسلوا أيديكم مسقطين غسلكم إلى المرافق فلو سلم ما ذكره زفر لم يضرنا لأن المرافق والكعبين حينئذ تخرج عين إسقاط الغسل فتدخل في الغسل وهو عين مرادنا وفيه بحث وهو أنه مع كونه مخالفاً لإجماع المفسرين مشتمل على تكلف مستغنى عنه. التقرير الثاني ما اختاره صاحب كشف Rather, the intended meaning is what we mentioned: that equality necessitates doubt, as testified by the commentator's phrase in 'Al-Tawdih'. This is in 'Al-Talwih'. And it was answered with several aspects: First: That the lack of knowledge of a proponent of inclusion is not conceded, because it is possible that the commentator was aware of it, but this is questionable, as mere rational possibility is insufficient in this matter. Second: That it was held by Abd al-Qahir al-Jurjani, unlike the second, which was held by Ibn Malik alone, and this is also questionable, because the first being a school of al-Jurjani, after the correctness of its transmission from him, does not remove its weakness, and the second being a school of Ibn Malik alone is false because grammarians explicitly state that it is the school of the majority. Third: That lack of knowledge of a proponent of the first does not necessitate its weakness; rather, it revolves around the evidence, and the evidence for inclusion is stronger, which is interpreting 'ila' as 'hatta' in that both indicate the end limit, and the chosen position in 'hatta' is inclusion of what comes after in what comes before, as stated by al-Zamakhshari in 'Al-Mufassal' and al-Radi in 'Sharh al-Kafiya', but this too is questionable, because that being chosen for 'hatta' does not necessitate it being chosen for 'ila', as there is a difference between them in some respect. Hence al-Radi said: 'The apparent view is that what comes after 'hatta' enters the ruling of what comes before it, unlike 'ila', where the apparent view is non-inclusion unless with a contextual indicator.' End. His statement: 'And what they mentioned...' – the well-known point in the books of usul scholars to prove the inclusion of the forearms and ankles in response to Zufar is that the limit in His statement 'to the elbows' and His statement 'to the ankles' is a limit of removal (ghayat al-isqat), and it has two formulations: The first formulation: that 'ila' in the aforementioned statement is not attached to 'washing' so as to be its limit and to exclude what comes after it from what comes before it, based on the principle that the limit does not enter under the ruling of the limited; rather, it is a limit for removal and attached to it. The estimation is: 'Wash your hands, removing your washing to the elbows.' So even if Zufar's point is conceded, it does not harm us, because then the elbows and ankles exit from the very thing of removal (i.e., the washing), so they enter into the washing, which is exactly what we intend. But there is a discussion: that besides being contrary to the consensus of exegetes, it involves unnecessary artifice. The second formulation: what the author of 'Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi' chose, and the author of 'Al-Talwih' considered it more appropriate: that if the beginning of the statement encompasses the limit, like the hand encompassing the elbow, then mentioning the limit there is to exclude what is beyond it from the ruling of what comes before it, not to extend the ruling to it, because it is already obtained without mentioning it. And if it does not encompass, then the limit is to extend the ruling to it, but it does not enter itself.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of the preposition 'ila' (to) in the Quranic verses of wudu (Quran 5:6): 'wa amsahu bi ru'usikum wa arjulakum ila al-ka'bayn' and 'wa aydiyakum ila al-marafiq'. The issue is whether the limit (elbows and ankles) is included in the washing or excluded. The majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that the elbows and ankles are included and must be washed, while Zufar argues for exclusion.

The passage begins by discussing a general principle: when doubt arises about exclusion after inclusion is established, the doubt does not overturn the inclusion. It then critiques the idea that 'ila' literally means inclusion, noting that such an opinion is weak and not held by most grammarians. The author refutes this critique with three responses, ultimately defending the inclusion argument based on analogizing 'ila' to 'hatta' (which commonly indicates inclusion). However, this analogy is shown to be imperfect, as scholars like al-Radi distinguish between the two prepositions.

The core argument for the majority is the 'ghayat al-isqat' (limit of removal) theory, presented in two formulations. The first formulation reanalyzes the syntactic structure: 'ila' is attached not to 'washing' but to 'removal' (isqat), meaning 'wash your hands, removing the washing up to the elbows.' Therefore, when Zufar argues that 'ila' excludes its object from the washing, the object (elbows) exits from the act of removal, thus entering into the washing itself. This clever inversion answers Zufar. The second formulation, attributed to the author of 'Kashf al-Asrar', distinguishes based on whether the initial part of the statement already encompasses the limit. If it does (e.g., the hand already includes the elbow as part of the hand), then mentioning the limit serves to exclude what is beyond. If it does not, then the limit extends the ruling. Since the hand does not naturally include the elbow, the latter applies, and the elbows are included.

The commentary thus clarifies the Hanafi position through detailed linguistic and usul analysis, showing how the majority's view is deeply grounded in interpretive reasoning.

Key Terms
غاية الإسقاط (ghāyat al-isqāṭ): A syntactic-legal term meaning 'limit of removal' or 'terminal of dropping'; it reinterprets the preposition 'ila' as attached to the action of removing the washing, so that the limit is not the object of washing but of removal, thereby including the limit in the washing.
إلى (ilā): A preposition meaning 'to' or 'up to'; used in the Quranic verses for wudu to indicate the limit of washing. The dispute centers on whether it excludes or includes the specified limit.
حتى (ḥattā): A preposition/phrase meaning 'until' or 'even'; used in Arabic grammar to indicate an end limit, with the common view (according to the text) being inclusion of what follows in the ruling of what precedes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first formulation of the ghayat al-isqat argument, what is the object of the preposition 'ila'?
A) The washing (ghusl)
B) The removal (isqat)
C) The hand (yad)
D) The limit (ghayah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority includes the elbows and ankles in wudu by arguing that 'ila' marks the limit of removal, not the limit of washing.

Two Explanations of Ghayat al-Isqat in Wudu
Explaining two interpretations of 'ghāyat al-isqāṭ' to refute Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi explanations for including the elbows and ankles in wudu and apply them to refute Zufar's argument.

Core Idea
The word 'ila' is interpreted as a 'goal of dropping' to include the elbows and ankles in washing.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi debate about the exact meaning of 'ila al-marāfiq' and 'ila al-ka'bayn' in the wudu verse (5:6). The author presents two scholarly explanations that both aim to prove that the elbows and ankles must be washed, contrary to Zufar's view that 'ila' excludes the mentioned parts. Understanding these explanations is essential for correctly applying the wudu ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the dispute is about whether the limit word 'ila' includes or excludes the object it mentions (elbows/ankles) from the washing command.
Zufar argued for exclusion; Abu Hanifa and the majority for inclusion.
2
Analyze the first explanation (taqrīr awwal)
Examine how 'ila' is treated as a 'goal of dropping' (ghāyat al-isqāṭ), meaning the limit applies to the dropping of washing, not the washing itself. The estimation: 'wash your hands while dropping your washing to the elbows' implies the elbows are washed because they are not part of the dropped washing.
This explanation is well-known but involves a paraphrase.
3
Analyze the second explanation (taqrīr thānī)
Understand the conditional approach: if the subject (e.g., hand) already encompasses the limit (elbow), then 'ila' excludes what is beyond; if not, 'ila' extends the ruling to include the limit. Here, 'hand' encompasses 'elbow' (one washes hand up to elbow), so 'ila' excludes beyond, but the elbow itself is already included by the subject.
This explanation is considered more precise and avoids unnecessary strain.
4
Apply the explanations to resolve the dispute
Conclude that both explanations support the mufta bihi position that elbows and ankles are wajib to wash, refuting Zufar. The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: failure to wash these parts invalidates wudu.
The majority view is operative for fatwa.

وما ذكروا أنها غاية الإسقاط. المذكورُ المشهور في كتب الأصوليين لإثباتِ دخول المرافق والكعبين رداً على زفر: أن الغاية في قوله: إلَى الْمَرَافِقِ، وقوله: إلى الكعبين، غاية الإسقاط، وله تقريران: التقرير الأول: أنّ «إلى» في القول المذكور ليست متعلقة بالغسل، حتى تكون غاية له، ويخرج ما بعدها عما قبلها بناءً على أنَّ الغاية لا تدخل تحت حكم المغيا، بل هي غاية للإسقاط، ومتعلّقة به. والتقدير: «اغسلوا أيديكم مسقطين غسلكم إلى المرافق»، فلو سُلّم ما ذكره زفر لم يضرنا؛ لأنَّ المرافق والكعبين حينئذ تخرج عين إسقاط الغسل، فتدخل في الغسل، وهو عين مرادنا، وفيه بحث، وهو أنه مع كونه مخالفاً لإجماع المفسرين مشتمل على تكلف مستغنى عنه. التقرير الثاني: ما اختاره صاحب كشف الأسرار شرح أصول البزدوي، وجعله صاحب «التلويح» أوجه من أنّ صدر الكلام إذا كان متناولاً للغاية، كاليد المتناول للمرافق، كان ذكر الغاية هناك لإسقاط ما وراءها عن حكم ما قبلها، لا لمد الحكم إليها؛ لأنه حاصل بدون ذكرها أيضاً، وإذا لم يكن متناولاً فالغاية لمد الحكم إليها فلا تدخل نفسها. فمشهور في الكتب، فلا نذكره.
And what they mentioned that it is the goal of dropping. The well-known mentioned in the books of usuliyyun to prove the inclusion of the elbows and ankles as a refutation of Zufar: that the goal in His statement: 'to the elbows', and His statement: 'to the ankles', is a goal of dropping, and there are two explanations for it: The first explanation: that 'ila' in the mentioned statement is not related to washing, such that it becomes a goal for it, and what comes after it exits from what precedes it based on the principle that the goal does not enter under the ruling of the goal-specified; rather it is a goal for dropping and related to it. The estimation is: 'Wash your hands while dropping your washing to the elbows', so if what Zufar mentioned is accepted, it does not harm us; because the elbows and ankles then exit from the essence of dropping the washing, so they enter into the washing, which is exactly our intent. And there is discussion in it, which is that along with it being contrary to the consensus of the exegetes, it contains unnecessary strain. The second explanation: what the author of 'Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi' chose, and the author of 'al-Talwih' made it more proper: that when the beginning of the speech encompasses the goal, like the hand encompassing the elbow, the mention of the goal there is to drop what is beyond it from the ruling of what precedes it, not to extend the ruling to it; because it is achieved without its mention as well. And when it does not encompass, then the goal is to extend the ruling to it, so it itself does not enter. So it is well-known in the books, so we do not mention it.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the interpretation of the word 'ila' in the Qur'anic verse on wudu (5:6). The author explains two scholarly explanations (taqrirān) to prove that the elbows and ankles are included in the washing, contrary to the claim of Zufar ibn Hudhayl. The first explanation treats 'ila' as a ghāyat al-isqāṭ (goal of dropping), meaning that the limit is not for the washing itself but for the dropping of washing; thus the washed part includes the limit. The second explanation, preferred by some later scholars, differentiates based on whether the beginning of the speech encompasses the goal (e.g., 'hand' encompasses 'elbow')—if it does, the goal is to exclude what is beyond, not to extend the ruling; if it does not, the goal extends the ruling. Both explanations aim to counter Zufar's argument that the limit excludes its own object. The author notes that the second explanation is well-known and declines to elaborate further.

Key Terms
غاية الإسقاط (ghāyat al-isqāṭ): A goal that drops something from the ruling of the preceding text; used here to argue that the limit itself is not subject to the washing command but its removal is.
غاية المد (ghāyat al-madd): A goal that extends the ruling to include the limit itself; the opposite of ghāyat al-isqāṭ.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'ila' always includes the limit.
B) Because the hand naturally encompasses the elbow, so the limit excludes only what is beyond.
C) Because the verse is understood as 'drop your washing to the elbows'.
D) Because Zufar's view is incorrect.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ila' in wudu is interpreted as a 'goal of dropping' to include the elbows and ankles, refuting Zufar's exclusion view.

Definition of Ka'b (Ankle) in the Hanafi School
Defining the term 'كعب' according to a riwaya of Hisham from Muhammad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ka'b and identify its exact location using the terms 'al-mufassal' and 'ma'qid al-shirāk'.

Core Idea
The ka'b is the middle foot joint at the shoelace knot — not the side bone.

This lesson unpacks a precise riwaya from Hisham ibn Muhammad al-Shaybani that defines the term ka'b. Knowing this anatomical detail is essential for correctly performing wudu, as the feet are washed up to the ka'b. This definition distinguishes the Hanafi school from others, and understanding it prevents common errors in ritual purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
Locate the word 'الكعب' (al-ka'b) in the text. Recognize that it is the term being defined by the author.
This is the central lexical item in the passage.
2
Extract the anatomical description
Find the phrase: 'هو المفصل الذي في وسط القدم' – 'it is the joint that is in the middle of the foot.' Note the use of 'المفصل' (joint) not 'العظم' (bone).
The Hanafi definition emphasizes the joint, not a protruding bone.
3
Understand the reference point
The text adds 'عند معقد الشراك' – 'at the place of the shoelace knot.' The 'شراك' is a strap on a sandal; its knot is on the top midline of the foot. This physically marks the ka'b.
The shoelace knot is tied over the middle joint, confirming its location.
4
Distinguish from other schools
Compare with the Shafi'i definition of ka'b as the two bones on the sides of the ankle. Note that the Hanafi definition is unique and based on this riwaya.
This difference affects the extent of foot washing in wudu.
5
Apply to wudu practice
Concretize: when washing feet in wudu, water must cover the entire foot up to and including the joint in the midline. Missing this area invalidates the washing.
Practical outcome: ensure water reaches the middle joint, not just the sides.

ثمَّ الكعب في رواية هشام عن محمد: هو المفصلُ الذي في وسط القدم عند معقد الشراك. قوله: عند معقد؛ أي موضع عقد الشراك وهو بالكسر: قطعة من الجلد ونحوه يعقد على النعل عرضاً، ويقال له بالفارسية: دوال نعل.
Then the ka'b in the riwaya of Hisham from Muhammad: it is the joint that is in the middle of the foot at the place of the shoelace knot. His statement: 'at the place of the knot'; that is, the place of tying the shoelace, which is (with kasra) a piece of leather or the like that is tied crosswise on the sandal, and is called in Persian: 'dool nal'.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage provides the Hanafi definition of the ka'b (ankle) as transmitted by Hisham from Muhammad al-Shaybani. The ka'b is not the protruding bone on the side of the foot (as some define), but rather the joint located in the middle of the foot at the point where the shoelace is tied. This definition is important for wudu, as the feet must be washed up to the ka'b. The commentary further explains 'ma'qid al-shirāk' as the place of tying the shoelace, which is a strap on sandals. This is a purely Hanafi definition, contrasting with some other schools that define the ka'b as the ankle bone on the side.

Key Terms
الكعب (al-ka'b): The ankle joint in the middle of the foot at the location of the shoelace knot.
الشراك (al-shirāk): A strap of leather tied crosswise on a sandal; the place of its knot marks the ka'b.

The Correct Definition of Ka'b in Wudu
Defining the correct meaning of ka'b for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi definition of ka'b and explain the linguistic proof from muqabala jam' bi jam'.

Core Idea
The ka'b is the prominent ankle bone, not the top joint — the dual form in the Qur'an proves two bones per foot.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the precise meaning of ka'b (ankle) required for valid wudu. While a famous but erroneous narration places it on the top of the foot, the Hanafi school, relying on the linguistic principle of muqabala jam' bi jam', establishes that ka'b refers to the prominent bone at the ankle. This lesson walks through the evidence and the refutation of the mistaken view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term 'ka'b' appears in the Qur'anic verse of wudu (5:6). The passage focuses on its precise meaning because it affects the validity of the ritual washing.
2
State the correct definition
The passage states that the 'most correct' (al-asahh) definition is that the ka'b is the prominent bone (al-'azm al-nati') at the ankle where the shin bone ends. This is the position of Al-Hidayah and the Zahir al-Riwayah.
The opposite view (joint on top of foot) is an error in transmission.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
Allah used plural forms for most limbs (faces, hands, elbows) but dual for 'ka'b'. The principle of muqabala jam' bi jam' requires that when a plural is contrasted with another plural, each individual receives one item. With dual, no such distribution occurs; instead it shows that each foot has two such bones.
This proves two ka'bs per foot, matching the two prominent ankle bones.
4
Reject the erroneous narration
The narration from Hisham stating the ka'b is on the top of the foot is identified as a mistake (sahw). Al-Ayni explains that Hisham confused a ruling from the chapter of Hajj (cutting khuffs) with Taharah.
Thus it is not a valid intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
In wudu, one must wash the feet including the prominent ankle bone. This differs from the Shafi'i position, which requires washing the joint on top of the foot.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi view; the opposite is not accepted.

It is famous in the books, so we do not mention it. Then the ka'b in Hisham's narration from Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the joint that is in the middle of the foot at the point where the strap is tied. So Zufar's statement to him: 'The limit does not enter the limited; if by it is meant the limit of extension, then it is correct, and if the limit of removal or more general than both, then it is a mistake.' The viewers differed on whether the commentator's intent here was the first or second exposition. The author of Hall al-Mushkilat and Hidayah al-Fiqh and others among the glossators inclined to the second, and Isam al-Din explained his words accordingly. Then it was objected that it was not another proof, but rather a choice for the fourth school with an explanation of the aspect of inclusion. The author of Hall al-Mushkilat answered that the commentator's purpose was the difference between the two expositions, even if their substance is one. But it is clear that the apparent meaning of his words implies that what he mentioned is a proof, and what they mentioned is another proof, not merely a difference in exposition. Abdullah al-Harawi and others chose the first exposition because it agrees with what is attested by the commentator's phrase in al-Tanqih. His words: 'So famous'—if by his words: 'It is the limit of removal' is meant being the limit of the deleted removal as mentioned by the proponents of the first exposition, then his words 'so famous' indicate his disapproval and weakening of it, as if he said: 'What I mentioned is the verification worthy of acceptance, and what they mentioned is famous with no basis of verification, so we do not mention it due to our disapproval.' Or if the second exposition is intended, then his words 'so famous' indicate repelling what is said: that he did not mention this exposition which others mentioned, but rather brought forward from himself another exposition, as if he said: 'Since that exposition was famous, we did not mention it, relying on its fame, for every new thing is pleasant.' His words: 'at the point where it is tied' means the place where the strap is tied, which (with kasra) is a piece of leather or similar tied crosswise on the sandal, called in Persian 'dawal-e na'l.' But the most correct is that it is the prominent bone to which the shin bone ends; and that is because Allah, exalted, chose the plural form in the parts of wudu, so it is intended by contrasting plural with plural. His words: 'But the most correct'—he said in al-Hidayah: 'The ka'b is the prominent bone; that is the correct.' End quote. Al-Ayni said in his commentary: 'By this he avoided what Hisham narrated from him that it is on the top of the foot, an error of Hisham in his transmission from Muhammad (peace be upon him); because Muhammad (peace be upon him) said in a discussion: They said that the Muhrim, if he does not find sandals, should cut his khuffs below the ka'bs, and he pointed with his hand to the place of cutting. Then Hisham transmitted it to the chapter of purity.' End quote. By this it is clear that the commentator's expression 'al-asahh' (most correct) which suggests that its opposite is also correct is not as it should be, and it is obligatory to use 'al-sahih' (correct) which indicates that its opposite is a mistake. Unless it is said that sometimes 'al-asahh' is used and 'al-sahih' is meant. His words: 'So it is intended...' etc.; the summary is that it is established in its place that contrasting plural with plural requires the distribution of individuals over individuals, as they say 'They rode their mounts', meaning each one rode his own mount. And contrasting plural with dual does not require that, as they say 'They wore two garments', meaning each of them wore two garments. If you understand this, we say: Allah chose the plural in the parts of wudu: the faces, heads, hands, and elbows. So the meaning according to the rule is that each of them should wash his face and his hand to the elbows, and wipe his head. And it is known that in each hand there is one elbow; due to contrasting the elbows with the hands. And He chose the dual form for the ka'b, so that meaning is not present here. Thus it indicates that in each foot there are two ka'bs, and the ka'b according to the meaning narrated by Hisham (peace be upon him) is only one in each foot; so it is necessary that the intended meaning is the prominent bone.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling from the commentary of Al-Wiqaya, clarifying the correct interpretation of 'ka'b' (ankle) for the validity of wudu. The author first notes that the famous opinion (that the ka'b is the joint at the top of the foot) is well-known and thus he does not repeat it. He then presents the view transmitted by Hisham from Imam Muhammad (which is identical to the famous one). This is followed by a discussion regarding Zufar's objection about whether the limit (ghaya) includes the limited, which is a meta-commentary on the textual reasoning. The correct view, however, is stated clearly: 'But the most correct is that it is the prominent bone to which the shin bone ends.' The evidence for this is linguistic: Allah used plural forms for the limbs of wudu (faces, hands) and used dual for 'ka'b'. According to the principle of 'muqabala jam' bi jam'' (contrasting plural with plural), the plural implies distribution of individuals, but dual does not. Since 'ka'b' is dual, it implies two bones per foot, which matches the prominent ankle bone (one on each side). The correct view is taken from Al-Hidayah, and Hisham's transmission is identified as an error (sahw) — he mistakenly transferred a ruling from Hajj to Taharah. The commentator then notes that the phrase 'al-asahh' (most correct) might incorrectly imply that the opposite view is also valid, whereas it should be 'al-sahih' (correct) to show the opposite is a mistake.

Key Terms
الكعب (al-ka'b): In this text, the ka'b is the prominent bone (al-'azm al-nati') at the ankle where the shin ends, as opposed to the joint on top of the foot.
مقابلة الجمع بالجمع (muqabala jam' bi jam'): A linguistic principle meaning contrasting a plural with another plural, which implies distribution of the items among the individuals.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines ka'b as the prominent ankle bone, proven by the dual form in the wudu verse and the linguistic principle of muqabala jam' bi jam'.
