The Ka'b in Wudu: Linguistic Proof from the Dual Form
Linguistic and legal argument for the correct definition of ka'b

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the linguistic rule of muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam' to derive the Hanafi position on the ka'b and evaluate the strength of the proof.

Core Idea
The dual form 'al-ka'bayn' in wudu proves two protruding ankle bones per foot, refuting the single-bone view.

In this passage from 'Umda al-Ri'ayah, the commentator refines the definition of the ka'b found in al-Hidayah. The key proof is linguistic: the Quranic verse of wudu uses plural for most limbs but dual for ka'b. Understanding the rule of muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam' (juxtaposing plural with plural) is essential to derive the correct meaning. You will learn how this rule leads to the conclusion that each foot has two ka'bs, and how the commentator defends this against objections and alternative narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on ka'b
State clearly what the Hanafi school holds: the ka'b is the protruding bone at the ankle, two per foot. Note that this is the mufta bihi position from al-Hidayah.
The opposing view (Hisham's narration) is considered an error.
2
Understand the linguistic rule: muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam'
This rule states that when a plural word is juxtaposed with another plural, it implies a division of individuals upon individuals. For example, 'they rode their mounts' means each person rode his own mount. But when plural is juxtaposed with dual, this division does not apply; instead, the dual applies to each individual of the plural.
The passage illustrates with 'they wore two garments' meaning each person wore two garments.
3
Apply the rule to the verse of wudu (5:6)
Allah uses plural for faces, hands, and forearms, but dual for ka'b (al-ka'bayn). By the rule, the dual does not distribute across individuals; rather, each foot must have two ka'bs. Thus the ka'b cannot be a single bone (as Hisham or the Shafi'i claim) but must be two protruding bones per foot.
The word 'al-ka'bayn' is dual, so it cannot mean one total, but two per foot.
4
Address the objection that the rule would imply washing only one hand and one foot
A critic objects that if the rule is applied, it would require washing only one hand and one foot because the plural of hands and faces would divide. The Hanafi response is that the rule is the default (asl) but can be overridden by external indicators (qara'in). Here, the mutawatir practice of the Prophet and consensus establish the obligation to wash both hands and both feet.
Similarly, the zakat verse is cited as a counterexample but answered with the same principle.
5
Evaluate the objection that the reasoning is purely rational and not based on linguistics or Sharia
Some critics claim the commentator is using rational proof to determine a linguistic meaning, which is unacceptable. The response clarifies that the purpose is not to prove the linguistic meaning rationally, but to show that Hisham's reported meaning cannot be the intended meaning of the verse. The proof is based on the Quranic text itself (a textual proof), not independent reason.
The passage calls the opposing arguments 'foolish words' and refers to al-Si'ayah for refutation.

والجواب عنه : أن رعاية هذه القاعدة هي الأصل إلا إذا وجدت قرينة خارجية تدل على عدم اعتبارها، فلا تعتبر، وقد يجاب عن الأول بأن اللازم ملتزم، ولا ضير فيه، فإنّ فرضيّة غسل اليد الأخرى والرجل الأخرى ثابتة بدلالة النص وبالأحاديث المتواترة وبالإجماع.

But the most correct (al-asahh) is that it is the protruding bone which the shin bone ends at; and that is because Allah Most High chose the plural form for the limbs of wudu, so it is intended to juxtapose plural with plural. [1] His statement: 'But the most correct'; said in al-Hidayah: 'The ka'b is the protruding bone, that is the correct (al-sahih) view.' End. (1) Al-Ayni said in his commentary: 'By this he guarded against what Hisham narrated from him that it is on the top of the foot. That is an error of Hisham in his transmission from Muhammad Allah; because Muhammad said in a issue: They said: Indeed, the pilgrim in ihram, if he does not find sandals, he cuts his khuffs below the ka'bs, and pointed with his hand to the place of cutting. So Hisham transmitted it to the chapter of purification.' End. And by this it becomes clear that the commentator's expression 'al-asahh' (most correct) which gives the impression that its opposite is also correct is not as it should be. Rather, the obligation is to use 'al-sahih' (correct) which conveys that its opposite is a mistake and error, unless one says that sometimes 'al-asahh' is used and what is meant is 'al-sahih'. (3) [2] His statement: 'so it is intended...' etc.; its summary: It has been established in its place that juxtaposing plural with plural necessitates the division of individuals upon individuals, like their saying 'they rode their mounts', meaning each one rode his mount. And juxtaposing plural with dual does not necessitate that, like their saying 'they wore two garments', for its meaning is that each one of them wore two garments. If you have understood this, then we say: Allah chose the plural in the limbs of wudu: i.e., faces, heads, hands and forearms. So the meaning, according to the rule, becomes: each one of them should wash his face and his hand up to the forearms, and wipe his head. And it is known that in each hand there is one forearm, because of the juxtaposition of forearms with hands. And He chose the dual form for ka'b, so that meaning (division of individuals) is not present here. So that indicates that in each foot there are two ka'bs, and the ka'b according to the meaning that Hisham narrated is only one in each foot, so it must be that what is intended is the protruding bone. Division of individuals upon individuals, and He chose the dual word for ka'b, so it cannot be intended to mean division of individuals upon individuals. So it is determined that the dual is facing each one of the individuals of the plural, so in each foot there are two ka'bs, and they are the two protruding bones, not the locus of the strap (ma'qid al-shirak), for that is one in each foot. [1] His statement: 'Division of individuals upon individuals'; it is objected against this that it would necessitate that what is prescribed is washing one hand and one foot, while it is not so. Also, this rule is contradicted by His statement: 'Take from their wealth charity', for observing it would necessitate the obligation of zakat on each individual property of each individual man, not what is in every property. And the answer to it is that observing this rule is the principle unless there is an external indicator indicating that it is not to be considered; then it is not considered. And it may be answered regarding the first objection that the necessary consequence is accepted and there is no harm in it, for the obligation of washing the other hand and the other foot is established by the indication of the text, by the mutawatir hadiths, and by consensus. [2] His statement: 'so it is'; i.e., there must be in a foot two ka'bs, not one ka'b, so that the juxtaposition of plural with the dual is correct; and the ka'b that Hisham mentioned is one in each foot, so it cannot be what is intended in the verse of wudu. And from here you know the refutation of what is objected against the commentator, that his reasoning to prove that ka'b in language means the protruding bone and nothing else is a proof of the linguistic meaning by rational evidence, and that is not permissible; rather what is considered in that is the explicit statements of the lexicographers. And if it is proving its meaning in the Sharia, then it is an establishment of a legal matter by opinion, and that is not permissible, for opinion has no entry in the legal rulings; rather what is considered is following the texts of the Sharia. The aspect of refutation is that his purpose is not that the meaning of ka'b is this in language or in Sharia; rather his purpose is to prove that the meaning narrated by Hisham cannot be intended in the verse of wudu. And there are for the observers here foolish words, we have shown their falsehood in al-Si'ayah. (1) al-Tawbah: from verse 103. (2) al-Si'ayah ala Sharh al-Wiqayah (1:70-71).
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) from 'Umda al-Ri'ayah on the definition of the ka'b in the context of wudu. The commentator refines the ruling found in al-Hidayah, which states that the ka'b is the protruding bone at the ankle. The correct position, according to the Hanafi school, is that there are two ka'bs per foot, not one. The passage begins by citing al-Hidayah that the ka'b is the protruding bone. It then addresses the narration from Hisham, who transmitted from Muhammad al-Shaybani that the ka'b is on the top of the foot. The commentator, following al-Ayni, explains that Hisham erred and misapplied a statement from Muhammad concerning the pilgrim in ihram cutting his khuffs below the ka'bs. The commentator criticizes the phrasing 'al-asahh' (most correct) because it implies the contrary view might also be correct, whereas the contrary view is actually erroneous. The main proof given is linguistic: the Quranic verse of wudu (5:6) uses plural for most limbs (faces, hands, forearms) but dual for ka'b. According to the principle of muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam' (juxtaposing plural with plural), each individual washes one of each limb. But with dual, the rule of division of individuals upon individuals does not apply; instead, the dual indicates two per foot. Thus, the ka'b must be two per foot, matching the protruding ankle bones, not the single strap locus on top. The passage then addresses two objections. First, if the division rule is applied, it would imply washing only one hand and one foot, but the obligation of washing both is established by other texts and consensus. Second, the rule seems contradicted by the zakat verse 'Take from their wealth charity', because applying the rule would require zakat on each individual property of each individual, whereas the rule is that zakat is on total wealth. The answer is that the rule is the default but can be overridden by external indicators (qarain). In wudu, the external indicator (the mutawatir practice) confirms washing all four limbs. Finally, the commentator refutes the objection that he is proving a linguistic or legal meaning by rational argument, clarifying that his intent is only to show that Hisham's reported meaning cannot be the one intended in the Quranic verse.

Key Terms
الكعب (al-ka'b): In this passage, it is proven to be the protruding bone at the ankle (العظم الناتئ), two per foot.
مقابلة الجمع بالجمع (muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam'): The linguistic rule that when plural is juxtaposed with plural, it necessitates the division of individuals upon individuals, i.e., each individual in the first plural corresponds to one individual in the second.
الأصح (al-asahh): The commentator critiques its use because it implies the opposite view is also correct; he prefers 'al-sahih' (correct) to emphasize that the opposite is erroneous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument in this passage, why does the dual form 'al-ka'bayn' in the verse of wudu indicate two ka'bs per foot?
A) Because the rule of muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam' does not apply to dual, so each foot must have two ka'bs.
B) Because the dual form always means two, and there is no other explanation.
C) Because the verse uses plural for other limbs, but dual for ka'b, so by the principle of muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam', each individual (foot) gets two ka'bs.
D) Because the Sahih hadith states that there are two ka'bs on each foot.

Key Takeaway
The ka'b in wudu is the protruding ankle bone, and each foot has two, proven by the Quranic dual form 'al-ka'bayn' and the linguistic rule of muqabala al-jam' bi al-jam'.

The Dual Form 'Ka'bayn' Proves Two Bones per Foot
Refuting objections to the dual meaning of ka'bayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proof that the dual form 'ka'bayn' in the wudu verse requires two bones per foot and explain how external evidence (qarina) overrides the default grammatical rule.

Core Idea
The dual 'ka'bayn' cannot refer to one bone per foot; it forces two per foot, supported by sunnah and ijma.

The wudu verse (5:6) commands washing the feet up to the 'ka'bayn'. Hanafis interpret this as two protruding bones per foot. This lesson unpacks the grammatical argument and addresses objections that the dual form would lead to absurdities. You will see how the Hanafi school uses linguistic default rules tempered by external evidence to reach a sound ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical claim
The commentator uses the dual form 'ka'bayn' – which should refer back to the plural 'arjulakum' – and argues that the default rule of 'division of individuals over individuals' would force each foot to have one bone, but the dual requires two, so the rule is overridden. Identify this claim in the first part of the passage.
2
Analyze the objection
Notice the objection: if we apply the default rule consistently, it would require washing only one hand and one foot (since 'aydi' and 'arjul' are plural), which is absurd. Also, the zakat verse (9:103) would imply zakat on each individual coin. Understand the force of the objection.
3
Study the Hanafi response
The response affirms that the default rule is the principle, but it is overridden by an external indicator (qarina) – here, the mutawatir hadiths and ijma that establish washing both hands and feet, and the sunnah that zakat is on the whole wealth. See how the Hanafi school uses qarina to limit the default.
4
Distinguish the purpose of the argument
The passage refutes the charge that the commentator is defining ka'b linguistically by rational proof. Instead, the argument only aims to show that Hisham's interpretation (one bone per foot) cannot be the intended meaning of the verse. Understand this methodological distinction.
5
Apply the ruling
Conclude that the Hanafi position – washing both protruding bones on each foot during wudu – is established by the dual form, supported by sunnah and ijma. This is the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the argument made by the commentator (likely from al-Hidayah) concerning the interpretation of the word 'ka'bayn' in the wudu verse (5:6). The commentator argued that the dual form 'ka'bayn' forces the meaning that there are two ankle bones per foot, because if the dual referred back to the plural 'feet', it would have to be divided among the feet, which is impossible for a dual. However, an objection is raised: applying this rule of 'division of individuals over individuals' would lead to absurdities, such as washing only one hand and one foot in wudu, or requiring zakat on each individual's small property. The answer is that this rule is the default but is overridden by external evidence (qarina) from the text, consensus, and hadith. Furthermore, the objection that the commentator is using rational proof to determine a linguistic meaning is refuted: his aim is not to define the term lexically or legally, but to show that Hisham's narration (that ka'b is a single bone) cannot be what the verse intends.

Key Terms
الكعب (al-ka'b): The protruding ankle bone; there are two per foot.
المثنى (al-muthanna): The dual grammatical form, indicating exactly two items.
قرينة (qarina): An external contextual indicator that modifies or overrides the default interpretation of a general or plural statement.

Key Takeaway
The dual form 'ka'bayn' in the wudu verse necessitates two protruding bones per foot, a ruling solidified by the use of external evidence from sunnah and ijma to override default grammatical rules.

Definition of Mash and Its Objections
Defining mash and responding to three objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mash (wiping), identify the three objections raised against it, and explain each response.

Core Idea
Mash requires passing the wet hand over the limb; mere contact is insufficient, and the restriction to hand is conventional.

This passage defines the act of mash (wiping) in the Hanafi school, clarifying its essential components and addressing three logical or linguistic objections. Understanding this definition ensures that you correctly apply the rules of wiping during wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of mash
Read the definition: 'al-mash is the touching of the wet hand to the limb, either with moisture taken from the vessel, or moisture remaining after washing a limb.' Note the two sources of moisture.
The definition is absolute and covers head, beard, splint, socks, etc.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
Objection: Mere touching is insufficient; passing (imrar) is required. Response: 'Touching' here implicitly includes passing; it is understood contextually.
This is the Hanafi position that imrar is obligatory.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
Objection: Grammatical inconsistency: 'touching' is an attribute of hand/water, while 'mash' is an attribute of the person. Response: Two resolutions: either interpret 'touching his hand' as an attribute of the wiper, or treat 'mash' as passive (the thing wiped) so touching is an attribute of the limb.
Both resolutions make the predication sound.
4
Analyze the third objection and response
Objection: Restricting to hand is problematic since one could wipe with the foot (valid but not sunna). Response: It is a conventional restriction based on common practice.
The definition uses 'hand' because that is the normal practice.
5
Note the source of moisture
The 'vessel' is conventional; water can come from any clean source like a river, as established by sunna.
The sunna is from authentic hadiths.

And the wiping of a quarter of the head and beard. 'Al-mash' is: the touching of the wet hand to the limb, either with moisture taken from the vessel, or with moisture remaining on the hand after washing a limb from the washed parts. [1] His statement: 'al-mash... etc.' – this is a definition of mash in an absolute sense, so as to include the wiping of the head, beard, splint, leather socks, and other than that. Objections are raised against it: First: There must be passing of the hand over the wiped part, and mere touching is not sufficient in our madhhab, as the commentator will clearly state soon. Its answer: The addition of 'touching' is understood in context, and the intended meaning is touching with passing. Second: Touching is an attribute of the hand and water, while mash is an attribute of the one wiping; so how can the predication be correct? Its answer: What is meant is the touching of his hand, i.e., of the one wiping, and the act of touching itself, though it is an attribute of the hand and water, yet the touching of his hand is an attribute of the one wiping necessarily. Or it is said that here 'mash' is in the passive form meaning the thing wiped, and the touching of the hand to the limb is an attribute of the wiped limb, so the predication is correct. Third: The restriction to 'hand' is problematic, because if one wipes with the foot, it is valid, though contrary to the sunna. Its answer: It is a conventional restriction based on the normal custom and well-known method. [2] His statement: 'He takes from the vessel' – meaning new moisture he takes from the vessel that contains the wudu water; the restriction is conventional, for if he performs wudu from a river, the ruling is the same. This is the established sunna from the action of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) as in Sahih al-Bukhari, Sahih Muslim, Sunan Abi Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and others. [3] His statement: 'or moisture' – meaning moisture that has remained on his hand after washing a limb like the face and hand; for...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling analysis. The commentator defines 'mash' (wiping) as touching the limb with a wet hand, specifying two sources of moisture: either new water taken from the vessel or leftover moisture from a washed limb. Three objections are raised and answered. First: that mere touching is insufficient; the answer is that 'touching' here implicitly includes passing (imrar). Second: a grammatical objection about attributing touching to the action of wiping; the answer presents two stylistic resolutions. Third: restricting to the hand is unnecessary since wiping with the foot is valid; the answer states it is a conventional restriction based on common practice. The passage then clarifies that the source of water is not restricted to a vessel; any clean water, like a river, suffices, as established by the sunna.

Key Terms
المسح (al-mash): Touching a limb with a wet hand with passing (imrar), using moisture from a vessel or leftover from washing.
الإصابة (al-isaba): The act of touching or reaching; in this definition it is used to mean touching with an active motion of passing.
الإمرار (al-imrar): The act of drawing or passing the hand over the part being wiped.
البلل (al-balal): Moisture; the water used for wiping, either newly taken or leftover.
الجبيرة (al-jabira): Splint; an example of something that can be wiped over in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the answer to the objection that restricting the definition to the hand is problematic because one could wipe with the foot?
A) Wiping with the foot is invalid.
B) The restriction is conventional based on common practice.
C) The foot is not considered a limb for wiping.
D) The definition is only for the head and beard.

Key Takeaway
Mash requires a wet hand with passing (imrar), and the restriction to 'hand' and 'vessel' are conventional, not absolute conditions.

The Hanafi Definition of Masah and Its Refutation of Objections
Commentator defines masah and answers three linguistic objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of masah and explain how three linguistic objections are resolved.

Core Idea
Masah requires passing the hand, not mere touching — definition refined by objections.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya defines the term 'masah' (wiping) as used in wudu and then defends the definition against three linguistic objections. The commentator clarifies that the definition includes all types of wiping (head, beard, splint, khuff) and ensures that the act is properly understood as the passing of a wet hand over the limb. Understanding these objections and their responses is key to grasping the Hanafi technical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the exact wording of the definition of masah given in the passage. Note that it includes both moisture taken from the vessel and moisture remaining after washing a limb.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection claims that mere 'touching' (إصابة) is insufficient because masah requires 'passing' (إمرار). See how the commentator responds by saying that 'touching' here implies passing.
This is the Hanafi position: mere wetting without passing is not enough.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection concerns the logic of the definition: how can 'touching' (an attribute of the hand) be equated with 'wiping' (an attribute of the person)? The commentator gives two answers: either the phrase 'touching of his hand' indirectly refers to the wiper, or the word 'masah' is considered passive (being wiped).
This is a linguistic technicality, but it shows the precision required in legal definitions.
4
Examine the third objection
The third objection questions why the hand is specified, since wiping with the foot is also valid (though against sunna). The answer is that the specification is incidental, based on custom (urf).
Custom does not restrict the definition's generality.
5
Conclude the Hanafi definition
After these responses, the definition stands: masah is the touching of a wet hand to a limb with passing, and the hand is mentioned by custom not by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسح ربع الراس واللحية) المسح : إصابة اليد المبتلة العضو، إِمَّا بَلَلاً يأخذه من الإناء، أو بَلَلاً باقياً باليد بعد غسل عضو من المغسولات ١ ] قوله : المسح ... الخ ؛ هذا تعريف للمسح مطلقاً بحيث يشمل مسح الرأس واللحية والجبيرة والخفّ وغير ذلك، وأورد عليه وجوه : أحدها : إنه لا بد في المسح من إمرار اليد على الممسوح، ومجرد الإصابة لا يكفي عندنا، كما سيصرح به الشارح عن قريب. وجوابه : إن إضافة الإصابة عهدية، والمراد الإصابة مع الإمرار. ثانيها : إنّ الإصابة صفةٌ لليد ،والماء والمسح صفة للماسح ، فكيف يصح الحمل. وجوابه : إنَّ المراد إصابة يده ؛ أي الماسح ونفس الإصابة وإن كانت صفة لليد والماء، لكن إصابة يده صفة للماسح بالضرورة، أو يقال : المسح هاهنا مبني للمفعول بمعنى الممسوحية، وإصابة اليد العضو صفة للعضو الممسوح، فيصح الحمل. ثالثها : إنّ التقييد لليد مخلّ ، فإنّه لو مسح بالرجل صح، وإن كان خلاف السنة. وجوابه : إنه قيد اتفاقي بناءً على العادة الغالبة والطريقة المعروفة.
And wiping a quarter of the head and beard: Wiping is the touching of the wet hand to the limb, either with moisture taken from the vessel, or with moisture remaining on the hand after washing a limb of the washed parts. [1] His statement: 'Wiping... etc.' This is a definition of wiping in absolute terms such that it includes wiping the head, beard, splint, and khuff and other than that. Objections are raised against it: First: That in wiping it is necessary to pass the hand over the wiped part, and mere touching is not sufficient according to us, as the commentator will clarify shortly. Its answer: That the addition of 'touching' is specific, and what is meant is touching with passing. Second: That touching is an attribute of the hand, and water and wiping are attributes of the wiper, so how is predication valid? Its answer: That what is meant is touching of his hand, i.e., the wiper, and the act of touching itself, even if it is an attribute of the hand and water, but touching of his hand is an attribute of the wiper by necessity, or it is said: Wiping here is passive in the sense of being wiped, and the touching of the hand to the limb is an attribute of the wiped limb, so predication is valid. Third: That the restriction to the hand is problematic, because if he wipes with his foot it is valid, though it is against the sunna. Its answer: That it is an incidental restriction based on prevailing custom and known practice.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'masah' (wiping) as used in wudu. The commentator clarifies that the definition includes wiping the head, beard, splint, and khuff. He then addresses three linguistic objections to the definition. Since the matn reads 'إصابة اليد المبتلة العضو' (touching the limb with a wet hand), the first objection states that wiping requires passing the hand, not mere touching. The commentator answers that 'touching' here is understood with passing, as the context implies. The second objection notes that 'touching' is an attribute of the hand, while wiping is an attribute of the person, so the definition would be illogical. The answer is that 'touching of his hand' is indirectly an attribute of the wiper, or that the word 'masah' is in the passive sense (being wiped), making the touching an attribute of the limb. The third objection questions why the hand is specified, since wiping with the foot is valid (though not sunna). The commentator explains that the specification is incidental, based on the usual practice of using the hand. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
المسح (al-masah): The act of touching a limb with a wet hand, with the intention of wiping, including passing the hand over the limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Passing the hand over the limb
B) Simply touching the limb with a wet hand
C) Using water from the vessel only
D) Wiping with the foot

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of masah requires passing the wet hand over the limb, with the hand mentioned by custom, and the definition holds against three linguistic objections.

Using Leftover Water from Washing for Masah
Ruling on using leftover moisture from washing for masah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using leftover water from washing for masah and explain the basis of the dispute between the majority and al-Hakim al-Shahid.

Core Idea
Water from washing is not musta'mal until it flows; leftover moisture is valid for masah.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the use of moisture remaining on the hand after washing a limb (e.g., the face) for wiping (masah) the head. The source explains the differing definitions of used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal) for washing vs. wiping, and settles on permissibility based on a prophetic hadith and the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: using moisture left on the hand after washing a limb for wiping the head. The initial ruling states that such moisture from a previously wiped limb is not sufficient.
2
Understand the Key Distinction
Grasp the Hanafi rule: water becomes musta'mal upon contact with the skin for wiped limbs, but only upon flowing for washed limbs. Thus leftover moisture from washing is not yet musta'mal.
This distinction is from Nawadir Ibn Rustam.
3
Examine the Dalil
Consider the hadith from Abu Dawud: the Prophet wiped his head from the leftover water in his hand. This indicates permissibility.
dalil_block: Sunnah_ahad, zanni thubut and dalala.
4
Map the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Identify the opposing positions: majority (Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn) permit, al-Hakim al-Shahid prohibits based on immediate musta'mal status.
madhhab_dispute_map defines the ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The majority position (permissibility) is relied upon for fatwa, as the mashayikh corrected al-Hakim al-Shahid.
authority_weight: fatawa_mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكفي البَلَلُ الباقي في يدِهِ بعد مسح عضو من الممسوحات، ولا بَلَل يأخذه من بعض أعضائه سواء كان ذلك العضو مغسولاً أو ممسوحاً، وكذا في مسح الخف. [١] قوله : البلل الباقي ... الخ ؛ وذلك لأن الماء بمجرد ملاقاة البشرة في الممسوحات يصيرُ مستعملاً، بخلاف الغَسل ؛ فإنَّ الماء فيه لا يصيره مستعملاً، ما لم يسل. كذا نقله في «المحيط» عن «نوادر ابن رستم». واعلم أن المفروض في مسح الرأس أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح ، وهو شعرة أو ثلاث شعرات عند الشافعي (1) المسح جائز ؛ لأنه ليس بمستعمل، وقد روى أبو داود أنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مسح رأسه (۲) من فضل كان في يده (٢). [١] قوله : البلل الباقي ... الخ ؛ وذلك لأن الماء بمجرد ملاقاة البشرة في الممسوحات يصيرُ مستعملاً، بخلاف الغَسل ؛ فإنَّ الماء فيه لا يصيره مستعملاً، ما لم يسل. كذا نقله في «المحيط» عن «نوادر ابن رستم».
And the moisture remaining on his hand after wiping a limb from the wiped parts is not sufficient, nor moisture taken from some of his limbs, whether that limb is washed or wiped, and similarly in wiping the khuff. [1] His statement: 'The remaining moisture... etc.' That is because water, by mere contact with the skin in wiped parts, becomes used (musta'mal), contrary to washing; for water in washing does not become used unless it flows. Thus it is transmitted in al-Muhit from Nawadir Ibn Rustam. And know that what is obligatory in wiping the head is the minimum to which the name of wiping applies, which is one hair or three hairs according to al-Shafi'i. (1) Wiping is permissible because it is not used water, and Abu Dawud narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wiped his head from the leftover water in his hand.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on using leftover water (بلل باقٍ) for masah. The matn states that moisture remaining after wiping a wiped limb (like the head) is not sufficient for another wipe, nor is moisture taken from a washed or wiped limb. The commentator explains that water becomes 'musta'mal' (used) upon contact with the skin in wiped limbs, but in washing (e.g., face, hands), it only becomes used when it flows off. This is from Nawadir Ibn Rustam. Then there is a mention of a hadith from Abu Dawud showing that the Prophet used leftover water from his hand to wipe his head, implying it is permissible. The commentator notes a disagreement among Hanafi scholars: al-Hakim al-Shahid held that such water is used and prohibits its use, while the majority of mashayikh (common scholars) considered it permissible. The dispute is intra-Hanafi, and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is permissibility, as per the hadith.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Water that has been used to fulfill a religious obligation (like wudu) and is no longer pure for that purpose.
بلل باقٍ (balal baqi): Leftover moisture remaining on the hand after washing or wiping a limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, unless one wipes the head before drying the hand

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits using leftover moisture from washing for masah because such water is not musta'mal until it flows, and this is supported by a prophetic hadith.

Minimum Amount for Wiping the Head: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute on minimum wipe for head

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the minimum wipe for the head and compare it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The fard in wiping the head is the minimum that can be called wiping — even a part of a hair suffices.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies the obligatory amount for wiping the head during wudu. It contrasts the Hanafi position, firmly rooted in linguistic principles and the Prophet's practice, with the Shafi'i view requiring three hairs. The commentator also notes that the three-hair opinion is considered atypical (shad) within the Shafi'i school itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses the fard (obligatory) amount of wiping the head. The Hanafi ruling is that any act linguistically called 'masah' is sufficient.
The act is wiping the head; the ruling is fard, but the extent is the minimum.
2
Locate the evidence
Identify the hadith cited (Sunan Abi Dawud) where the Prophet wiped his head from leftover water without specifying a quantity. This is used as proof for the Hanafi position.
The hadith supports the principle that no specific amount is required.
3
Compare with the opposing school
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i is reported to require three hairs. The commentator remarks that this opinion is 'shad' (atypical) and not the relied-upon view in the Shafi'i school.
Some Shafi'is adopt the minimum as well, but the dominant view is three hairs.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis can validate wudu with the slightest wipe (even a part of a hair), whereas Shafi'is must ensure at least three hairs are wiped.
This affects daily practice for followers of each school.
5
Understand the response to the opposing view
The Hanafi response relies on linguistic analysis (the word 'masah' applies to any amount) and the absence of a specified quantity in the primary sources.
The commentary in al-Binaya confirms this Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المفروض في مسح الرأس أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح ، وهو شعرة أو ثلاث شعرات عند الشافعي (1) المسح جائز ؛ لأنه ليس بمستعمل، وقد روى أبو داود أنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مسح رأسه (۲) من فضل كان في يده (٢). [٢] قوله : أدنى ما يطلق ؛ هذا هو المعتبر من مذهبه من أنَّ الفرض أدنى ما يطلقُ عليه اسم المسح ، ولو كان بعض شعرة، وقدره بعضهم بالشعرة ؛ أي بمقدارها ، وبعضُهم بثلاث شعرات، وهو قول شاد في مذهب الشافعي . كذا في «البناية).
And know that what is obligatory in wiping the head is the minimum to which the name of wiping applies, which is one hair or three hairs according to al-Shafi'i. (1) Wiping is permissible because it is not used water, and Abu Dawud narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wiped his head from the leftover water in his hand. [2] His statement: 'The minimum that is called...' This is the relied-upon view of his school: that the obligatory act is the minimum to which the name of wiping applies, even if it is part of a hair. Some of them specified it as one hair, i.e., its amount, and some as three hairs, which is an atypical opinion in the Shafi'i school. Thus in al-Binaya.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage clarifies the fard (obligatory) amount for wiping the head in wudu. The Hanafi school holds that any act that can linguistically be called 'masah' is sufficient, even if it involves just a part of a hair. This is based on the principle that the obligation is satisfied by the reality of the term. The Shafi'i position, according to the commentator, is that three hairs are needed, though he notes this is a 'shad' (atypical) opinion within the Shafi'i school; some Shafi'is require one hair. The proof for the Hanafi view is the same hadith (Prophet wiping from leftover water), which does not specify a quantity. The commentator in al-Binaya confirms this. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that a minimal wipe is valid, while the Shafi'i position (as presented) is more restrictive.

Key Terms
أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح (adna ma yutlaqu 'alayhi ism al-masah): The minimum that can be linguistically called 'wiping', such as passing the hand over a few hairs.

The Obligatory Amount of Head Wiping: Hanafi, Shafi'i, and Maliki Positions
Inter-madhhab dispute on minimum head wiping amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the obligatory amount of head wiping, explain the proofs of the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Maliki qiyas argument.

Core Idea
Wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory in Hanafi fiqh; the minimum suffices for Shafi'i; the full head for Maliki.

This passage presents the classical inter-madhhab dispute on the obligatory amount of head wiping (mash) in wudu. The commentators compare the Hanafi view (quarter of the head) with the Shafi'i view (any part) and the Maliki view (full head). Each school derives its ruling from the same Quranic verse (5:6) but with different interpretive principles. The passage focuses on the Hanafi refutation of the Maliki analogy with tayammum, setting the stage for the Hanafi proof from the hadith of the forelock.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanafi views on the obligatory amount of head wiping.
Shafi'i: any part; Maliki: full head; Hanafi: quarter.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Understand the absolute (mutlaq) nature of the Quranic command 'wipe your heads' that leads to the minimum.
No restriction to part or whole.
3
Examine the Maliki proof
Understand the analogy (qiyas) with tayammum based on the same preposition 'bi'.
Requires full coverage by analogy.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Note the objection that tayammum is a branch, not a root, and the Hanafi counter that the verse is ambiguous (mujmal) and clarified by hadith.
Qiyas invalid; bayan from hadith.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference in validity of wudu: a Hanafi's wudu is valid with a quarter, a Shafi'i's with any part, a Maliki's only with full head.
Affects validity of prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكفي البَلَلُ الباقي في يدِهِ بعد مسح عضو من الممسوحات، ولا بَلَل يأخذه من بعض أعضائه سواء كان ذلك العضو مغسولاً أو ممسوحاً، وكذا في مسح الخف.
واعلم أن المفروض في مسح الرأس أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح ، وهو شعرة أو ثلاث شعرات عند الشافعي. وعند مالك : الاستيعاب فرض كما في قوله : فَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ . وعندنا ربع الرأس. وقد ذكروا أنه إذا قيل : مسحتُ الحائط بيدي ، يراد به كله، لأن الحائط اسم للمجموع، وقد وقع مقصوداً؛ لأنَّه محل، و المحل هو المقصود بالفعل المتعدي، فيراد به كله.
[١] قوله : عملاً بإطلاق النص؛ يعني إنّما قال الشافعي بفرضية الأدنى لإطلاق قوله : ﴿وَامْسَحُوا بِرُهُ وسِكُمْ } ، فإنه ليس فيه تقييد لا بالكل ولا بالبعض، والمطلق يحمل على الأدنى لتيقنه.
[۲] قوله : كما في قوله : إشارة إلى دليل مالك ، وهو أنَّ النص الوارد في مسح الرأس كالنص الوارد في التيمم بقوله : فَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ ؛ لدخول الباء فيهما على الممسوح، وقد فرض استيعاب الوجه في التيمم، فيفرض استيعاب الرأس أيضاً في المسح. وأورد عليه : أنَّ التيمم فرع ، والوضوء أصل ، وقياس الأصل على الفرع غير ملائم. وجوابه : إنه لما وقع الخفاء في آية الوضوء حملناها على آية التيمم تفصيلاً وبياناً لحد المسح ، ووجه الاشتراك كونهما طهارة، ومثل هذا لا يسمى قياساً ، بـل بـيـاناً وتفصيلا.
[٣] قوله : وقد ذكروا ؛ دليل على عدم اشتراط الاستيعاب، فالغرض منه نفي مذهب مالك ، وأما إثبات مذهب أصحابنا فموقوف على تقدير الإجمال في الآية ، وجعل حديث الناصية بياناً له على ما سيجيء ذكره.
And the moisture remaining on his hand after wiping a part of the wiped parts is not sufficient, nor is moisture he takes from some of his limbs, whether that limb is washed or wiped. Similarly in wiping the khuff. And know that the obligatory act in wiping the head is the minimum to which the name of wiping applies, and it is one hair or three hairs according to al-Shafi‘i. And according to Malik, complete coverage is obligatory, as in His saying: 'Wipe your faces.' And according to us, it is a quarter of the head. And they mentioned that when it is said, 'I wiped the wall with my hand,' it refers to the whole of it, because the wall is a name for the whole, and it is intended; because it is a place, and the place is what is intended by the transitive verb, so it means the whole of it. [1] His statement: 'Acting upon the absolute nature of the text' means that al-Shafi‘i only said that the minimum is obligatory due to the absolute nature of His statement 'And wipe your heads,' for there is no restriction in it, either to the whole or to a part, and the absolute is interpreted as the minimum due to certainty. [2] His statement: 'As in His saying' is a reference to Malik's proof, which is that the text regarding wiping the head is like the text regarding tayammum in His saying: 'Wipe your faces'; because the letter ba enters upon what is wiped in both, and complete coverage of the face in tayammum has been made obligatory, so complete coverage of the head in wiping is also obligatory. It is objected: Tayammum is a branch, and wudu is a root, and analogy of the root upon the branch is not suitable. The answer: Since there was ambiguity in the verse of wudu, we interpreted it in light of the verse of tayammum as elaboration and explanation of the limit of wiping, and the common factor is that both are purifications, and such a thing is not called analogy, but rather elaboration and explanation. [3] His statement: 'And they mentioned' is evidence for not requiring complete coverage, so the purpose of it is to negate Malik's school. As for establishing our school's position, it depends on assuming that the verse is ambiguous and making the hadith of the forelock an explanation of it, as will come.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya. The author presents the three major positions on the obligatory amount of head wiping in wudu: Shafi'i (minimum, even a hair), Maliki (full head), and Hanafi (quarter of the head). He begins by stating a rule that leftover moisture after wiping one part cannot be reused for another, then proceeds to the main dispute. For Shafi'i, the proof is the absolute command 'wipe your heads' (Quran 5:6) without specifying quantity, so the minimum suffices. For Malik, the similar imperative in tayammum (wipe your faces) requires full coverage, and by analogy head wiping should also be full. The Hanafi response rejects this analogy because tayammum is a secondary purification (badal) and wudu is primary; instead, the ambiguity in the wudu verse is resolved by the hadith of the forelock (which indicates wiping part of the head). The Hanafi position is that a quarter of the head is obligatory, based on a different hadith and the linguistic principle that 'wipe' can apply to a part. The passage refutes Malik's view and sets up the Hanafi proof to follow.

Key Terms
مسح (mash): Wiping, i.e., passing a wet hand over a limb without water flowing.
استيعاب (isti'ab): Complete coverage of the entire part (head).
الربع (al-rubu'): Quarter, here meaning one-fourth of the head area.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire head
B) A quarter of the head
C) Any part, even a single hair
D) Three-fourths of the head

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires wiping at least a quarter of the head in wudu, based on a hadith that clarifies the Quranic command, distinguishing it from the Shafi'i and Maliki views.

Dispute on the Extent of Wiping the Head: Hanafi vs Maliki
Hanafi rebuttal of Maliki view on head wiping extent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory, identify the linguistic and analogical proofs used by both schools, and critique the Maliki argument for comprehensive wiping.

Core Idea
Wiping a quarter of the head is obligatory because the preposition 'ba' in 'wipe with your heads' indicates partiality, not totality.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding the obligatory amount of head wiping during wudu. The central argument hinges on the interpretation of the preposition 'ba' in the Quranic verse. You will learn how Hanafi scholars use linguistic principles and the hadith of the forelock to establish that only a quarter of the head is required, and how they refute the Maliki analogy with tayammum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State clearly that the Hanafi school holds wiping a quarter of the head as obligatory. Note that this is based on the verse 'wa amsahu bi ru'usikum' and the explanatory hadith of the forelock.
The quarter is the minimum that satisfies the command.
2
Identify the Maliki opposition and its proof
The Maliki school requires wiping the entire head (isti'ab). Their main proof is the tayammum verse 'fa amsahu bi wujuhikum', where comprehensive wiping of the face is required, and the 'ba' enters on the locus similarly.
They use qiyas between wudu and tayammum.
3
Analyse the Hanafi linguistic argument
The Hanafi response relies on the different meanings of 'ba' when attached to the instrument versus the locus. When 'ba' enters on the locus (as in 'bi ru'usikum'), it indicates partiality; when on the instrument, it indicates totality of the wiped object.
This is the core of the dispute: whether 'ba' indicates tab'id (partiality) or is extra.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the tayammum analogy
The text argues that tayammum is a branch (far') and wudu is a root (asl), so qiyas of the root on the branch is invalid. Instead, the tayammum verse is used as a clarification (bayan) of the ambiguity in the wudu verse, not as an analogy.
This reframes the Maliki proof as a valid explanatory tool, not a binding qiyas.
5
Assess the critique of 'ba' as extra
Some grammarians and Malikites consider the 'ba' in the tayammum verse as extra (za'idah). The text dismisses this as weak because positing an extra letter without necessity is not methodologically sound.
This step reinforces the Hanafi reliance on the 'ba' having real meaning.

[2] His statement: 'so its comprehensive wiping is not established'; know: First, that the letter 'ba' comes for fourteen meanings as detailed by Ibn Hisham in 'Mughni al-Labib' and its commentators, among them: adhesion, assistance, partiplicity, circumstantiality, etc. The apparent meaning from Sibawayh's statement is that it is literal in adhesion and metaphorical in others, and this is chosen by a group of investigators, among them Ibn al-Humam in 'Tahrir al-Usul'. Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligatory amount of wiping (mash) the head during wudu. The text presents the Hanafi position that only a quarter of the head is obligatory, contrasting with the Maliki position that requires wiping the entire head. The discussion begins by stating the Maliki view that comprehensive wiping (isti'ab) is obligatory, citing the tayammum verse (فَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ) as evidence. The Hanafi response is that only a quarter is needed, and then delves into a linguistic argument about the preposition 'ba' (ب). The author explains that when one says 'I wiped the wall with my hand,' the whole wall is intended because the hand is the instrument and the wall is the locus. However, when one says 'I wiped with the wall' (using 'ba' on the locus), only part of the wall is intended because 'ba' typically indicates a means or instrument, which is not intended in its entirety. This principle is applied to 'وَامْسَحُوا بِرُءُوسِكُمْ' (wipe with your heads), where the 'ba' enters on the head (the locus), thus only part is required. The text then addresses the Maliki analogy with tayammum, pointing out that tayammum is a branch and wudu is the root, so analogy from branch to root is invalid. Instead, the tayammum verse is used to clarify the ambiguity in the wudu verse. The passage further critiques the view that the 'ba' is extra (za'idah), a view attributed to some grammarians and the Malikites, stating it is weak because it posits an extra letter without necessity. Finally, it mentions that the Hanafi school relies on the hadith of the forelock (hadith al-nasiyah) as an explanation of the verse, which makes the obligation limited to a quarter. This commentary follows the order of the text and only explains what is present.

Key Terms
باء (baa): The preposition 'ba' (ب) is analyzed for its meaning when attached to the wiped object. According to Hanafi scholars, it indicates partiality (al-tab'id) when the object is the locus, as in 'بِرُءُوسِكُمْ'.
مسح (mash): Moist wiping with the hand over a part of the body. In wudu, it is the act of wiping the head with wet hands.
استيعاب (isti'ab): Comprehensive wiping, i.e., covering the entire surface of the head.
تبعيض (tab'id): Partiality; indicating a part of something.
زائدة (za'idah): Extra or redundant letter that does not affect meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Wiping the head is not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi requirement to wipe a quarter of the head in wudu is derived from the linguistic principle that the preposition 'ba' on the locus indicates partiality, supported by the explanatory hadith of the forelock.

The Meaning of 'Ba' in the Tayammum Verse: Hanafi Linguistic Defense
Critical examination of the meaning of 'ba' in the tayammum wiping verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi linguistic argument that the preposition 'ba' indicates adhesion (ilṣāq) and thus partial wiping suffices, and critique the Maliki view that 'ba' is extra and requires full wiping.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school proves that 'ba' means adhesion, not extra or full coverage, so wiping a part of the head fulfills the obligation.

The precise meaning of a single Arabic preposition—al-bā'—determines a fundamental difference in wudu practice between madhahib. In this passage, the Hanafi commentator refutes the Maliki claim that 'ba' is extra (zā'idah) and instead demonstrates through linguistic principles and prophetic practice that 'ba' indicates adhesion (ilṣāq), which does not require full coverage of the head. Understanding this argument is essential for appreciating how Hanafi scholars derive fiqh from the subtle nuances of the Arabic language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four opinions on 'ba' in the verse
List the four interpretations: extra (zā'idah), partitive (tab'īḍ), instrumentality (istiʿānah), and adhesion (ilṣāq).
The commentator notes that most exegetes choose adhesion.
2
Understand the Hanafi preference for adhesion
Recognize that Hanafi scholars, following Sibawayh, consider adhesion as the literal meaning; other meanings are metaphorical.
Ibn al-Humam in Tahrir al-Usul also supports this.
3
Refute the Maliki 'extra' claim using hadith
The Prophet (ﷺ) wiped only his forelock (nāṣiyah) as reported by al-Mughirah, proving that 'ba' is not extra.
If 'ba' were extra, the command would require full wiping, contradicting the Prophet's action.
4
Trace the reasoning from adhesion to partiality
The purpose of a transitive verb like 'mash' is to bring the action into contact with the object, not to cover it entirely. Thus, 'ba' indicates the locus of contact, which may be a part.
This linguistic principle is key to establishing partiality without requiring 'ba' itself to mean partiality.
5
Apply the argument to the fiqh ruling
Conclude that wiping a part of the head (a quarter, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view) fulfills the wajib of wiping the head.
The minimum is a quarter per Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn consider a finger's width sufficient.

الإكرام والمسح من الفاعل، وإلا لكان ذكر المفعول لغواً، وكان الفعل منزلاً منزلة اللازم، بل إيقاعه على المفعول وإلصاقه به ، وهذا لا ينافي ما ذكروا أنَّ المفعول به فضلة، والعمدة إنما هو الفاعل، فإنَّ ذلك باعتبار نفس حقيقة الفعل.
And if it is said: 'I wiped the wall with (bi) it', what is meant is part of it, because the principle with the preposition 'ba' is that it enters upon means (wasā'il), and they are not intended, so full coverage of them is not established; rather, what suffices is what enables attaining the intended object. So when 'ba' enters upon the place (of wiping), the place is likened to means, hence full coverage of the place is not established. His statement: 'because the principle' is the cause for the difference mentioned, i.e., when 'ba' enters upon the object of wiping, part of it is meant; when it enters upon the instrument, the entire object of wiping is meant and part of the instrument. His statement: 'so full coverage of them is not established'. Know: First, the letter 'ba' has fourteen meanings as detailed by Ibn Hisham in 'Mughni al-Labib' and its commentators, among them: adhesion (ilṣāq), instrumentality (istiʿānah), partitive (tabʿīḍ), circumstance (ẓarfiyyah), etc. The apparent statement of Sibawayh is that it is literal in adhesion and metaphorical in others, which is chosen by a group of investigators, including Ibn al-Humam in 'Tahrir al-Usul'. Second, they differed concerning 'ba' in His statement: 'so wipe your faces' into opinions: First: it is extra, because the verb 'mash' is transitive by itself to its object; this is the school of Ibn Jinni among grammarians, as related by al-Radi, and the Malikis inclined to it, but it is weak, because choosing an extra letter without necessity while the meaning stands without it is not the way of investigators. If they say: That is shown by the action of the Prophet (ﷺ) for he wiped his entire head as established by the narration of Abdullah ibn Zayd in Sahih al-Bukhari, Muslim, Sunan al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and al-Tirmidhi, and by the narration of Ali and al-Miqdam and others in Sunan Abu Dawud. We say: It is established from him the sufficiency of wiping the forelock (nāṣiyah) as well, which is the front of the head, by the narration of al-Mughirah in Muslim, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, al-Tahawi, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, and al-Tabarani, and likewise by the narration of Anas in Abu Dawud and al-Hakim. This indicates that 'ba' is not extra, but rather for tabʿīḍ (partiality) or what leads to its meaning. The second opinion: it is for tabʿīḍ, and the Shafi'is inclined to it. The third: it is for instrumentality, so the implied meaning is 'wipe your heads with water', so in the verse there is deletion and inversion, and the artificiality in that is not hidden. The fourth: it is for adhesion, which is the most correct and chosen by most exegetes. Third: the reasoning (taqrīr) presented by the commentator for denying full coverage is only needed by one who takes 'ba' as for instrumentality or adhesion. As for one who takes it as for tabʿīḍ, there is no need for it. The best way is to take 'ba' as for adhesion and to establish partiality through this reasoning. Clarification of the commentator's reasoning depends on laying down premises: First: the purpose in a transitive verb like wiping and the like is to convey the action from the doer to the object and to bring it about upon it, so the locus of the action's occurrence is the object, intended. Do you not see that in our saying 'I honored Zayd' and 'I wiped the wall', the mere occurrence of honor and wiping from the doer is not intended; otherwise, mentioning the object would be superfluous and the verb would be treated as intransitive. Rather, it is to bring the action about upon the object and to adhere it to it. This does not contradict what they mentioned that the object is extra (faḍlah) and the essential is the subject, for that is with regard to the reality of the verb itself.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on a commentary of al-Wiqayah. It delves into the grammatical interpretation of the preposition 'ba' in the Quranic verse on tayammum (5:6). The author argues that the Hanafi position that wiping a part of the head suffices is derived from the linguistic analysis of 'ba'. He begins by noting the principle that 'ba' when used with means (wasā'il) does not require full coverage of the means. Then he lists the fourteen meanings of 'ba', citing Sibawayh and Ibn al-Humam, noting that the literal meaning is adhesion (ilṣāq) and others are metaphorical. He then presents four opinions on 'ba' in the verse: (1) it is extra (zā'idah) – the Maliki view, which he refutes by citing hadith where the Prophet wiped only his forelock, proving it is not extra. (2) It is for tabʿīḍ (partiality) – the Shafi'i view, which he notes but does not fully embrace. (3) It is for instrumentality (istiʿānah) – which requires a strained interpretation. (4) It is for adhesion (ilṣāq) – chosen by most exegetes. He then reconciles adhesion with partiality by explaining that when 'ba' enters upon the object of a transitive verb like 'mash', the purpose is to bring the action into contact with the object, not to cover all of it. Thus, the 'ba' indicates the locus of contact, which may be a part. This linguistic reasoning, supported by the hadith of wiping the forelock, establishes that wiping a part of the head is sufficient. The author follows the order: first the principle, then the opinions, then the refutation of the extra 'ba' claim, then the reconciliation of adhesion and partiality. The Hanafi position is that the 'ba' is not extra; it indicates that the wiping action is attached to the head, but full coverage is not required.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bā'): The Arabic preposition used in the verse; its meaning determines the extent of wiping required.
التبعيض (al-tab'īḍ): Indicating a part of the whole; that the action applies to only a portion of the object.
الإلصاق (al-ilṣāq): Adhesion; that the preposition indicates the action is attached to the object.
الاستيعاب (al-isti'āb): Full coverage; that the action must encompass the entire object.
الزائدة (al-zā'idah): Grammatically extra or redundant; having no semantic function.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position as defended in this passage, why does the preposition 'ba' in 'famsahu bi wujuhikum' not require full coverage of the head?
A) Because 'ba' is extra and has no meaning.
B) Because 'ba' indicates adhesion (ilṣāq) and the purpose of a transitive verb is to bring the action into contact with the object, not to cover it entirely.
C) Because 'ba' means 'with' and the water is the instrument, so only the hand needs to wipe the head.
D) Because the verse has a hidden inversion and deletion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's position that wiping a part of the head suffices in wudu is grounded in the literal meaning of 'ba' as adhesion, the nature of transitive verbs, and the prophetic practice of wiping the forelock.

Hanafi Argument for Partial Wiping of the Head
Linguistic and legal analysis of the ba' in wudu verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument for partial wiping of the head and evaluate how the ba' in the wudu verse supports this view.

Core Idea
The ba' in 'wamsahū biru'ūsikum' indicates partiality or adherence, so wiping a part of the head suffices.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author defends the Hanafi position that wiping only a part of the head (such as the forelock) fulfills the obligation of masah in wudu. The argument hinges on the linguistic analysis of the preposition ba' in the Quranic verse and is supported by hadith evidence. By tracing the four possible meanings of the ba' and the premises about transitive verbs, you will see how the Hanafi school derives its ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi school's stance: wiping any part of the head (e.g., the forelock) suffices for the fard of masah.
The relied-upon view requires a quarter, but the argument here supports any part.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i and Maliki schools require wiping the entire head. Their primary evidence is the hadith where the Prophet wiped his whole head.
Also, their understanding of the ba' often leads to full coverage.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent cites narrations from Abdullah ibn Zayd, Ali, and others showing the Prophet wiping his entire head.
These are authentic and must be addressed.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response cites hadith of the Prophet wiping only his forelock (nāṣiyah) and provides a linguistic analysis of the ba' showing it can indicate partiality or that the wiped object becomes like a tool.
The author lays down four premises about transitive verbs and means.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis may wipe only a part (at least a quarter), while Shafi'is and Malikis must wipe the entire head.
This difference affects daily wudu practice.

كتاب الطهارة الوضوء
فإن قالوا : تبين ذلك بفعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فإنه مسح على رأسه بتمامه كما ثبت برواية عبد الله بن زيد في صحيح البخاري ومسلم وسنن النسائي وأبي داود وابن ماجه والترمذي، وبرواية علي والمقدام وغيرهما في سنن أبي داود.
قلنا : ثبت منه الاكتفاء بمسح الناصية أيضاً، وهو مقدم الرأس برواية المغيرة عند مسلم والنسائي وأبي داود والطحاوي والبيهقي والدارقطني والطبراني، وكذا برواية أنس عند أبي داود والحاكم، فهذا يدل على أنَّ الباء ليست بزائدة، بل هي للتبعيض، أو ما يؤدي مؤداه.
والثاني : إنها للتبعيض وإليه جنحت الشافعية. والثالث : إنّها للاستعانة، فالتقدير: «وامسحوا رؤوسكم بالماء»، ففي الآية حذف وقلب، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التكلّف.
ورابعها : إنّها للإلصاق، وهو الأصح الذي اختاره أكثر المفسرين.
وثالثاً : إنّ التقرير الذي أورده الشارح لنفي الاستيعاب إنما يحتاجُ إليه من حمل الباء على الاستعانة أو الإلصاق، وأمَّا مَن حملها على التبعيض فلا حاجة إليه، وأحسنُ المسالك حمل الباء على الإلصاق، وإثبات التبعيض بهذا التقرير، وتوضيح تقرير الشارح موقوف على تمهيد مقدمات : الأولى : إنّ الغرض في الفعل المتعدّي كالمسح ونحوه هو إيصال الفعل من الفاعل إلى المفعول وإيقاعه عليه، فيكون محلّ إيقاع الفعل هو المفعول به مقصوداً. ألا ترى أن في قولنا : «أكرمتُ زيدا» و «مسحت حائطاً» ليس المقصود مجرد صدور الإكرام والمسح من الفاعل، وإلا لكان ذكر المفعول لغواً، وكان الفعل منزلاً منزلة اللازم، بل إيقاعه على المفعول وإلصاقه به، وهذا لا ينافي ما ذكروا أنَّ المفعول به فضلة، والعمدة إنما هو الفاعل، فإنَّ ذلك باعتبار نفس حقيقة الفعل.
الثانية : إن الوسائل تكون غير مقصودة؛ إذ المقصود في المتعدي إنما هو إيقاع الفعل على المفعول، وفي اللازم مجرّد صدوره عن الفاعل، سواء كان بهذه الوسيلة أو غيرها، ألا ترى إلى أنَّ الغرض الأصلي من : مسحت الحائط باليد»، أو : «ضربت زيداً بالعصا» إنَّما هو الإخبار عن إيقاع المسح على الحائط، والضرب على زيد، وأما كونه بالعصا أو باليد فأمر زائد عليه.
الثالثة : إنّ الأصل في الباء إذا كانت للاستعانة والإلصاق أن تدخل على الوسائل، أما على التقدير الأول فلأنَّ الاستعانة إنّما تكون بالوسائل، وأما على الثاني؛ فلأنَّ الملصق هو المقصود، والملتصق به يكون كالآلة في تحصيل الإلصاق. ومن هنا قالوا في باب البيع : «إنّ ما دخلت عليه الباء يكون ثمناً، والخالي عنها بيع». الرابعة : إنّ الوسائل لكونها غير مقصودة بنفسها لا يراد بها كلها، بل بقدر ما يحصل به أصل المقصود، ولعدم الاحتياج إلى استيعابها، ألا ترى أنَّ في قولنا : «ضربت زيداً بالعصا»، لا يراد بالعصا كلّه، بل بقدر ما يحصل به الضرب، وهذا هو الأصل، فإن خالفه كلام ما، كان ذلك لقرينة خارجية.
إذا عرفت هذا كله فنقول : إنه إذا قيل : «مسحتُ الحائط باليد» بإدخال الباء على الوسيلة كما هو أصلها، يراد بالآلة بعضها، وبالحائط كلّه؛ لكونه اسماً للمجموع. وإذا قيل : «مسحت اليد بالحائط لا يراد بالحائط كلّه؛ لأنَّ الباءَ لما دخلت عليه صار مشابهاً للوسيلة؛ لكون أصل الباء أن تدخل عليها، فكما يكتفي من الوسيلة على قدر الحاجة، يكتفي من المحل المشابه لها عليه. ومن المعلوم أن قوله تعالى : فَأَمَسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ ) دخلت فيه الباء على الممسوح، فكأنه قيل : وامسحوا الأيدي برؤوسكم»، فشابه الرأس فيه الآلة، فلا يثبت استيعابه، بل يراد به بعضه، فبطل مذهب مالك لله، هذا غاية التوضيح مع التنقيح.
The Book of Purity: Wudu. If they say: That is proven by the action of the Prophet, for he wiped his entire head as established by the narration of Abdullah ibn Zayd in Sahih al-Bukhari, Muslim, Sunan al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi, and by the narration of Ali and al-Miqdam and others in Sunan Abu Dawud. We say: It is also established that wiping only the forelock (the front part of the head) sufficed from him, by the narration of al-Mughirah in Muslim, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, al-Tahawi, al-Bayhaqi, al-Daraqutni, and al-Tabarani, and likewise by the narration of Anas in Abu Dawud and al-Hakim. This indicates that the ba' is not extra, but rather for partiality, or what conveys its meaning. The second (meaning): It is for partiality, and the Shafi'is inclined to it. The third: It is for instrumentality, so the estimation is 'Wipe your heads with water,' so in the verse there is deletion and inversion, and its artificiality is not hidden. The fourth: It is for adherence, which is the soundest view chosen by most exegetes. Thirdly: The explanation that the commentator presented to negate comprehensiveness is only needed by one who takes the ba' as for instrumentality or adherence; as for one who takes it for partiality, there is no need for it. The best approach is to take the ba' as for adherence and to establish partiality through this explanation. The clarification of the commentator's explanation depends on laying down premises: First: The purpose in a transitive verb like wiping and similar is to convey the action from the doer to the object and to effect it upon it, so the locus of the action is the object intended. Do you not see that in our saying 'I honored Zayd' and 'I wiped the wall,' the mere occurrence of honor and wiping from the doer is not intended; otherwise mentioning the object would be futile and the verb would be treated as intransitive. Rather, it is effecting it upon the object and adhering it to it. This does not contradict that the object is considered extra (fadlah) and the main thing is the subject, for that is with regard to the reality of the verb. Second: Means are not intended in themselves; the intention in transitive verbs is only the effecting of the action on the object, and in intransitive verbs merely its occurrence from the doer, whether with this means or another. Do you not see that the primary purpose of 'I wiped the wall with the hand' or 'I struck Zayd with a stick' is to inform about effecting the wiping on the wall and the striking on Zayd; as for it being with a stick or with the hand, that is something additional. Third: The principle for the ba' when it is for instrumentality and adherence is that it enters upon means. On the first interpretation, because instrumentality is only with means; on the second, because that which adheres is the intended object, and that to which it adheres becomes like the tool in achieving adherence. Hence they said in the chapter of sale: 'What the ba' enters upon becomes the price, and what is free of it is the commodity.' Fourth: Means, since they are not intended in themselves, are not intended entirely, but only to the extent that the original purpose is achieved, because there is no need to encompass them all. Do you not see that in our saying 'I struck Zayd with a stick,' not the entire stick is intended, but only the amount that achieves the striking. This is the principle; if an expression contradicts this, that is due to an external indicator. Once you understand all this, we say: When it is said 'I wiped the wall with the hand' by entering the ba' on the means as is its principle, part of the tool is intended, and the entire wall is intended because it is a noun for the whole. When it is said 'I wiped the hand with the wall,' the entire wall is not intended because when the ba' enters upon it, it becomes similar to the means, since the original function of the ba' is to enter upon it. So just as one suffices with the means to the extent needed, one suffices with the locus that is similar to it. And it is known that in His saying 'So wipe your faces' (Qur'an 5:6), the ba' entered upon that which is wiped, so it is as if it were said 'wipe your hands with your heads,' so the head becomes similar to the tool. Hence its entirety is not established, but rather a part of it is intended. Thus the school of Malik is nullified. This is the utmost clarification and refinement.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a detailed linguistic and legal argument defending the Hanafi position that wiping only a part of the head (e.g., the forelock) suffices for the obligation of masah in wudu. It begins by responding to an opponent who claims that the Prophet's wiping of his entire head proves the necessity of full wiping. The Hanafi response cites hadiths where the Prophet wiped only his forelock (nāṣiyah), showing that partial wiping is sufficient. This leads to a discussion of the meaning of the letter ba' in the Quranic verse 'wamsahū biru'ūsikum'. The author lists four possible meanings: extra (za'ida), partiality (tab'īḍ), instrumentality (isti'ānah), and adherence (ilṣāq). Most exegetes prefer adherance, but the author argues that regardless of which meaning is adopted, the Hanafi position that a part suffices stands. The Shafi'is are said to lean toward partiality, yet they require full wiping; the author implies this is inconsistent. The passage then provides a series of premises (muqaddimāt) about transitive verbs and means. The conclusion uses an analogy from tayammum ('famsaḥū biwujūhikum') where the ba' enters on the wiped object, making it like a tool, so only part is intended. Therefore, wiping the entire head is not required, and the Maliki view (which presumably demands full wiping) is invalidated. Throughout, the teacher emphasizes that the Hanafi position is based on both hadith and sound linguistic reasoning.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bā'): The preposition 'ba' in the verse, whose meaning is debated: extra, partiality, instrumentality, or adherence.
التبعيض (al-tab'īḍ): Partiality; that the ba' indicates taking a part of the object.
الناصية (al-nāṣiyah): The forelock or front part of the head; what the Prophet wiped in some hadiths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Extra (za'ida)
B) Instrumentality (isti'ānah)
C) Partiality (tab'īḍ) or adherence (ilṣāq)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's position on partial head wiping is supported by a rigorous linguistic analysis of the ba' and authentic hadith evidence.

The Linguistic Argument for Partial Head Wiping in Wudu
Linguistic analysis to refute Maliki position on head wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi linguistic argument using the preposition 'ba' to prove that wiping only part of the head is wajib in wudu, and evaluate the Maliki counterargument.

Core Idea
When 'ba' enters on the wiped object, it becomes like an instrument, requiring only partial coverage.

The Quranic verse 'fa'msahu bi ru'usikum' has been interpreted differently by the Hanafi and Maliki schools. The Hanafis rely on a nuanced linguistic analysis: the preposition 'ba' normally indicates the instrument, so when it attaches to the object of wiping, that object is treated as a tool, implying only partial wiping. This lesson unpacks that argument, addresses the Maliki objection, and examines the tayammum exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic rule about instruments
The author establishes that instruments (wasā’il) are not intended in their entirety; only the portion needed to achieve the action is intended. For example, in 'I struck Zayd with the stick,' only part of the stick is used.
This principle applies to all instrumental actions.
2
Analyze the function of the preposition 'ba'
The original role of 'ba' when used for assistance (isti‘ānah) and adhesion (ilṣāq) is to enter upon the instrument, not the object. Example: 'I wiped the wall with my hand' (ba on 'hand', the instrument).
This sets up the analogy for the wudu verse.
3
Apply to the wudu verse 'fa'msahu bi ru'usikum'
In this verse, 'ba' enters on 'heads' (the object of wiping). According to the rule, when 'ba' enters on the object, that object becomes like an instrument, so only part of it is intended. Hence, only a portion of the head needs to be wiped.
The Hanafi position is that wiping one-fourth of the head is sufficient.
4
Address the Maliki objection and the tayammum counterexample
The Maliki school interprets the verse as requiring full head wiping. An objection arises from the tayammum verse 'fa'msahu bi wujuhikum', where 'ba' also enters on the faces, yet Hanafis require full facial wiping. The Hanafi response: the full wiping in tayammum is established by separate evidence (the word 'minhu' or Sunnah), which overrides the general linguistic rule.
This shows that the rule can be superseded by stronger evidence.
5
Conclude with the practical difference
The Hanafi position reduces the obligation to a quarter of the head; the Maliki requires full coverage. This difference affects daily wudu practice.
Both views are based on valid ijtihad.
